R3ME. March 3.â a. m.â lat
est news from the Vatican is that
the Pope's temperature at mid
night had fallen several- points.
It appears that his disinclination
to observe the recommendations of his
physicians forms. an obstacle in the way
of prompt recovery. He has full confi
dence in his own strength and does not
readily acquiesce in their advice not to
move.
During the afternoon he Insisted upon
leaving his bed without assistance and
read several telegrams. The rise in his
temperature, which had given some
anxiety, was probably caused in part in
this way.
The doctors exclude everybody from
his apartments except his personal at
tendants. Professor Mazzoni remained
in readiness during the evening to pro
ceed to the Vatican if needed, but up
to 11 o'clock he had not been called,
and at that hour he did not expect to
go until daybreak.
The Pope said during the day: "It Is
a miracle of God that I am still alive;
for He wishes to spare His church a
terrible crisis at the present moment."
At 5 o'clock in the afternoon the doc
tors made a prolonged visit lasting
more than an hour, which caused some
uneasiness, but on leaving they gave
reassuring reports, Professor Mazzoni
saying there was not the least fear
that the pleura, bronchial tubes or
lungs would be affected. The Pope's
nephews also visited him in the course
of the afternoon and left his chamber
.much pleased with his progress.
Despite these optimistic reports,
grave doubts are entertained as to the
issue of the illness. His Holiness, it
seems, displays great interest in the
matter, and has asked the doctors as to
the best way of preserving the cyst,
explaining that he intended, when he
shoul.d have recovered, to dissect it for
. purposes of study.
Cardinal Ledochowski. the Prefect of
the Congregation of the Propaganda,
consulted yesterday with Cardinal
Creglia, Dean of the Sacred College,
Camorlingo of the Holy Roman Church
and Prefect of the Congregation of
Ceremonial and Cardinal Mertel, Vice
Chancellor of the Holy Roman Church
and First Cardinal Deacon, who, in con
junction with Cardinal Ledochowski,
according to the apostolic constitutions,
' assumes the government of the church
in case of the Pope's death. *
When the doctors proposed applying
â¢the clinical thermometer yesterday his
Holiness said that there was no need
â¢ to do so, as he could himself feel thai
the fever had disappeared, just as he ]
could feel the high temperature prior !
to the operation.
The Pontiff would not allow the
- wound to be sewed up, so the doctors
' had to be content with drawing the
ends together in place.
After the wound had been dressed,
chatting quietly with Dr. Mazzoni, the
Pope smilingly said: "I want you to
cure me without any pain and in two
days."
The surgeon replied In the same
spirit: "Your Holiness must not ex
pect me to perform miracles."
â¢ The Pope for the present will partake
only of liquid nourishment, such as
' soup, raw eggs and watered wine. The
doctors positively deny that, there is
any dysentery or danger of blood poi
soning. ;
Dr. Mazzoni to-day said to a high
personage at the Vatican who asked
for confidential information on the sub
ject that if the Pope recovered, as he
fully expected, there was no reason why
. he should not live for another ten
years, so great is his vitality.
' " 9-30 p. mâ The Pope Is very cheerful,
â¢ and In an interested way asked the doc
tors from time to time what they
'thought of his condition. They reas
sured him. There has, however, been a
slight increase in his temperature, to
which the doctors attach some import
ance, although hoping it is only due
the nourishment he has taken. Dr.
""Lapponi remained in attendance at the
Vatican all night. \
CONSOLIDATION OF ALL
SMELTERS AND REFINERS
One of the Most Gigantic Trusts in
the United States Has Been
Arranged. \
PITTSBURG, March Th* Post to
morrow will say: One of the most gigan
tic combinations yet attempted, by com
mercial organizations in the United States
was completeed to-day over the Wires be
tween New York and Pittsburg It is
â¢ a consolidation of all the smelttrs and
> refiners of precious [ metals In the United
Stats. The final move was the purchase
of the works of the Pennsylvania Lead
: Company of Pittsburg and the Pennsyl
vania Smelting Works of Salt Lake City. '
The San Francisco Call.
Utah. The price paid is not made known.
The capitalization of the new combine will
be $46,000,000. Officers will be elected April
10. It is understood that ex -Governor J.
B. Grant of Colorado will be president.
The deal has been under negotiation for
several months. The names of the firms !
in the combination follow: The Omaha
and Grant Smelter Company, with works
at Omaha, Denver and Durango, Colo.;
the Consolidated Smelting and Refining
Company of Kansas City, Mo., with
works at Kansas City, Leadvilie,
Colo., and El Paso, Tex.;- the United
States Smelting and Refining Com
pany of Chicago. with works at j
Chicago. Pueblo. Colo., Helena, Mont., and
Great Falls. Mont.: the Aurora Smelting
and Refining Company, with works at
Aurora, 111.; the Pueblo Smelting and
Refining Company, with works at Pueblo,
Colo.; the Philadelphia Smelting and Re
fining Company, with works at Pueblo, '
Colo.; the Globe Smelting and Refining
Company, with works at Denver, Colo.:
the Germania Smelting and Refining Com*
pany, with works at Salt Lake City; tne
Bimetallic Smelting Company, with works
at Leadvilie, Colo.; the Pennsylvania
Smelting Company, Salt Lake City, Utah, j
and the Pennsylvania Lead Company of j
Pittsburg.
BOLIVIA'S PRESIDENT
IS SORELY PRESSED
LIMA, Peru, March 2.â The position of
President Alonzo in Bolivia appears from
the latest news received here -to be be
coming embarrassing.
Colonel Adams, went south from here
on last Saturday's steamer and it is re
ported that he took with him 5000 rifles
and four pieces of field artillery, intend
ed for the Federalists, the name borne
by the revolutionists, who are sorely
pressing the President of Bolivia,
CAUGHT AFTER TWO YEARS.
LOS ANGELES, March 2.â William Cor
onado, who, on July 4, 1897, stabbed an
other Mexican to death at a celebration
near here, was captured in this city to
day. Coronado escaped after the mur
der and. has never been apprehended un
til the present time. He was taken to
San Fernando to-day and will be ar
raigned before a Justice of the Peace.
The officers had abandoned hope of ever
finding him.

SAIST FRANCISCO, FRIDAY, MARCH 3, 1899.
HON. CECIL RHODES
ARDENTLY ADVOCATES
AMERICAN IMPERIALISM
â  â  *.'--V , â â .â¢:â¢'â  i . . :-;, : â  â¢'â  â¢> iVv.-V.
The Caesar of South Africa Wants the
United States to Follow His Lead.
ALEXANDRIA, Egypt. March 2.â
Cecil Rhodes, who, in the eyes of
most Englishmen, Is the incarna
tion of the imperialistic policy,
holds views concerning the future
of the United States even more far
reaching that most American imperial
ists have broached. Mr. Rhodes, in
conversation with a representative of
the Associated Press on board . the
steamer Hapsburg on the Mediterran
ean, by which he came to Egypt in the
interest of the Cape to Cairo Railroad
and Telegraph line, predicted most con
fidently that within a century the
United States would have advanced the
work begun in the evacuation of Cuba
until it controlled all of . the American
hemisphere except Canada. The sub
stance of the conversation is repro
duced without, absolute verbal ac
curacy.
Mr. Rhodes expressed admiration of
the work already done in Cuba and pre
dicted that it would be carried on in
the Philippines. The United States he
considered' one of the nations best
equipped for colonization and repeated
ly exclaimed:
"You are taking to it like moth
er's milk, apparently with, the great
est delight."
He inquired what were the argu
ments of the opponents of imperialism
in the United States, and commented
that they seemed to be animated by
selfishness.
"It is the duty of civilized nations to
take charge of the barbarians and give
them a white man's government," he
said. "The United States is one of the
great powers and cannot escape this
duty."
He did not believe that the United
States would ever withdraw its
authority from Cuba, and thought the
manner in which Spain had been
evicted from her colonies and the
United States had taken possession
most businesslike. America's action in
compensating Spain for the Philip
pines surprised him.
"I would have bundled the Spaniards
out and made them pay a war
indemnity," was his. comment.
He declared that the United States
could never withdraw from the Phil
ippines because it was their duty to
give these people a strong and good
government, nor did he believe that
when they began to realize the results
of their work there Americans would
desire to abandon the policy. They
might grumble for a time over the ex
pense, hut that would be a "mere flea
bite" to a nation so rich. Why not
i abandon some of the expenses of the
Copyrighted, 1599, by the Associated Press.
pension list, which seems so unreason
ably large, if economy was necessary.
Even if a large navy must be built, the
United States could well afford one. So
far as the fear of provoking foreign en
tanglements and wars went, while Eng
land'and the United States stood to
gether and maintained their present
understanding, which was practically
an alliance, no combination of powers
dare menace them. To govern colonies
the United States must organize a staff
of, colonial officials, hut they would do
that as easily as they wpuld build a
new navy.
To the suggestion of the anti-im
perialists that . the Philippines would
be only the beginning of colonization,
he said:
"There are no more' islands of the sea
to be acquired. They are all taken up.
The territory of the savage races is
practically ail pre-empted by the civil
ized. The ultimate work of the United
States would be to govern South Amer
ica. You will probably begin with
Mexico, acquire the Central American
States, then those on the continent of
South America, until you hold all the
country to the south of you. These
states have incompetent governments â
practically barbarian governments â
and ft will be your duty to give them
the white man's government. Mexico
is well governed, but a nation cannot
depend upon one man and when Diaz
is dead it may be ruled no better than
before his time. Southward is the logi
cal direction of the expension of the
United States.
"The Philippines came to you by ac
cident; these neighboring peoples it will
be logically your duty to provide for."
When Blame's efforts to arrange a
friendly compact between the North
and South American republics were
mentioned Mr. Rhodes called them vis
ionary.
"You will possess all those states by
force of arms, and that within a cen
tury," he said, emphatically.
Asked if Canada would' logically be
included in this expansion, he said
Canada had a good Government and
therefore there was no need lor change.
Summarizing America's policy, he
said:
"You people of the United States
cannot always remain within your
selves. You cannot always go on
making money. You must get out Into
the world and' take up your share of
the world's burdens. Already you are
outgrowing your country, and you will
absorb other countries and you will
give them good government. . Why
should you not? You have it in your
blood. The Philippines will furnish
new -careers for your young men. The
whole work will strengthen you and
broaden your national character. Yes,
already you are taking to it like
mother's milk."
RED MARROW FROM
BONES OF THE DEAD
With That One of Koch's Pupils Will
Vanquish Dread- Pneu
monia.
NEW YORK, March 2.â A special
cable to the World from Berlin says:
Professor Wasserman, one of Professor
Koch's ablest pupils, is believed to have
discovered a new serum for the cure of
pneumonia. He inoculated, rabbits
with the bacillus which is generally be
lieved to cause pneumonia, and with
the serum thus gained he inoculated
mice suffering from pneumonia. A
subsequent series of experiments
showed it was in the red marrow of the
bones that antitoxin is produced, and
that red marrow taken from a human
corpse after death from pneumonia and
used as a serum will cure mice in
fected with that disease. Accordingly
it is hoped, that this serum will have
the same satisfactory results in human
beings. "
CHEAP FARES ON
THE ATLANTIC LINERS
Rates as Low on the German Lloyd
Boats as the Law
Allows.
LONDON, March The Daily Mall an
nounces that the North German Lloyd
has reduced the transatlantic passage
rate to Â£10 sterling even for express
steamers, which is the lowest rate the law
allows for passengers treated as saloon
passengers. If less were paid the passen
ger would be subjected to medical inspec
tion and would not be allowed to buy
spirits and the like.

THE REINA MERCEDES
AT LAST RAISED
SANTIAGO DE CUBA, March 2.â After
a long delay the former Spanish cruiser
Reina Mercedes, which was sunk in San
tiago harbor during the bombardment by
the American fleet on June 6, has been
raised and pumped out, the Government
tugs assisting the wrecking company. She
was brought up to the city this afternoon.
Such repairs as can be readily effected
will be made here, after which she will
probably be towed to Havana, though
final orders as to her movements have
not yet been received.

CALL HEADQUARTERS, SACRAMENTO, March 2.â a

"In spite of the charges that have been made
against meâ every possible charge that the press

The Grand Jury of Sacramento County returned twelve in
dictments against Burns for the fraudulent application of State
funds in his charge during the time he was Secretary of State. He
was arrested and imprisoned. In the course of the trial, in which
Burns' only defense was an attempt to fasten the crimes of which
he was accused on a deputy who had been selected by him for hi*
proficiency in political and other roguery, Burns admitted that he
had deliberately falsified the books of his office to cover up the
shortage in his accounts. Here is his admission, made under oath:
"I took the memorandum cash book and
changed these figures or amounts. I did not
change the figures, but where there would be
$13 25 I marked $9 75, and I took this cash book
back to him (Deputy Gale) after the Bth day of De
cember some timeâ it must have been three or
four days afterward."
This is one of the charges that have been made against
Burns. If it is not wrong to falsify the books. of a public office
for the purpose of concealing a shortage in the. public funds, then
Dan Burns, the Southern Pacific Company's candidate for United
States Senator, has done no wrong.

To put It in plain terms Burns and

Here is the pledge taken by all of the

"Resolved, That "we pledge ourselves
to vote for Colonel D. M. Burns for
United States Senator to succeed Hon.
Stephen M. White until by a majority

members a Senator shall be nom
inated."
Heretofore Burns has ruled his sup
porters with the iron hand of a master
and they have obeyed every order with
the blind faith of slaves. But recently
there have been murmurs of discontent
and threats of insurrection, and realiz
ing the danger impending Burns was
forced to call his men together for the
first time since the beginning of the
session and make known his wishes by
word of mouth instead of through the
medium of his lieutenants. It was an
evidence of weakness en the part of
Burns, but it had to be done in order
to enable him to stem the growing tide
of discontent and make it possible for
him to carry out his threat to prevent
the election of a Senator in case the
Legislature refused to elect him.
The caucus was held immediately af

sembly at 12:30 o'clock. It was called

in to Assemblyman Dibble, and he in

was then circulated among the Burns
followers to meet their chief in caucus

ly after adjournment. Word was also
sent to Major Frank McLaughlin, who
was downtown, to come to the Capitol

CALL HEADQUARTERS, SACRA
MENTO. March _.â Burns is busy

ticular have a deep and earnest'con
cern in the Senatorial contest. The fact
that California ought to have a Senator

Burns. He thought only of himself and
his desire for vindication.
He talked of a caucus, when knowl
edge'of party custom should teach him
that he has no right to submit his name
to a party caucus, and a caucus, should
one be called, would have no right to
consider his peculiar claims. According
to his own statements made to his fol
lowers he did not enter the contest in
good faith to serve the people, but en
tered the race to punish the press and
get even on his enemies. Had the an
nouncement been made by himself, be
fore, the members of the Legislature
were voted for by the people, that he
would be a candidate for United States
Senator he might with some degree of
fairness now ask that his name be sub
mitted with those of other candidates
for caucus consideration. But his' as
pirations for the office of Senator were
not disclosed until after the election.
In fact his motives in this respect were
hidden from the people. If the people
had believed that the Republican Leg
islature would commit the political folly
of seriously voting for him for Senator
the Republican ticket headed by Henry
T. Gage for Governor, down to the last
man on the legislative ticket, would
have been beaten out of sight. And
Burns has the effrontry to demand a
caucus of the people's* representatives
after he had concealed from the people
the fact that he designed to become a
candidate. ".'â¢';_ :': '-
Burns to-day has made his one speech
of the Senatorial campaign, and when
the people of California read and con
sider it they will comprehend that there
is nothing in his claims for the Sena
torship beyond a selfish desire to get
even with his assailants. It is not the
time in the history of this State and
this nation to send a man to the United
States Senate solely because that man
has enemies to punish and friends to
reward. Burns' own admissions testify
to the low plane on which his canvass
is conducted. He is now shaking his
fist at the people of California and say
ing, "A caucus must be held to con
sider my claims or no Senator shall
he elected by this Legislature." ,

official record. He made the record for
himself and should stand by it or fall
by it. If he desires to be vindicated
why does he not pay into the State
treasury the sum of 531,000 belonging to
the State, which was stolen from his
office while he was Secretary of State?
Men had better repent and make resti
tution before demanding vindication.
The course of D. M. Burns in this
Senatorial contest and the defiant
threat of his henchmen that this State
shall not be represented in Congress
until he is vindicated are calculated to
arouse the people irrespective of party
to a sense of responsibility in the
premises.

day Burns did not advance- any claims
for promotion to the high office of
United States Senator as a reward for
meritorious service rendered by him
self to the State or to the Republican
party. He could not put forth such
a claim. For all that he ever did for
the cause of Republicanism the party
has given him rewards and emolu
ments far beyond his desert. It made
him Secretary of State and it made
him Police Commissioner of San Fran
cisco. After such reward he bolted
the party nominations in San Fran
cisco and joined the non-partisan
movement with his Tocaloma Club
and defeated Republican candidates
for the Legislature. His bolting course
gave the Legislature to the Democrats
six years ago and gave that party a
Senator when the Republican party was
fairly and honestly entitled to the re
i ward. Again Burns is ready to defeat
the will of the Republicans of Cali
fornia by placing obstacles in the way
of the election of any Senator.
Pay of the members of the Legisla
i ture ended to-day, and the "Medicine
I men" are beginning to be apprehensive
of future rewards. It is expected by
the touts that the number of. votes re
quired for a choice of Senator will
diminish daily until the Legislature
adjourns. The Grant, Barnes and
Scott men are fully apprised of â¢ the
expectation and will hold their forces
to the finish.

FRAGRANT COYOTE
BILL HAS PASSED
THE SENATE
A Scathing Analysis of the
Measure by One Familiar
With Its Provisions,
CALL HEADQUARTERS, SACRA
MENTO, March 2.â The coyote scalp j
bill of fragrant memory has passed the
Senate and is now ready to be called
up in. the Assembly. It carries an ap
propriation of $257,615, and also con
tains provisions which, it is claimed,
gives the speculators and syndicates (
holding bounty certificates a decided j
advantage over the individual claim
ants. It is acknowledged that there
| are some just and honest claims mixed
in with bunches of fraudulent certifi
cates.
The Call has received the following
opinion of the pending measure from
one who has studied its provisions:
It robs the State. ' â ' .
It furnishes no relief to small claim
ants.
It enriches the speculators who have ac
cumulated blocks of fraudulent <.ia.....7,
and in whose interests substitute for ben
ate bill 227 has been cunningly drawn.
It would overwhelm the Attorney Gen- j
eral with a multiplicity of actions which j
he could never find time to defend; or if
he had the time it would involve enor- I
mous expense and create a corresponding
deficiency and be abortive as to any re- :
sults beneficial to the State or to those
deserving the benefits of legislation.
In short, the State would be at the
mercy of "holders" and honest owners
would be given no remedy.
A volume might be written upon the I
iniquities apparent upon the face of toe j
substitute, which in some inscrutable
way has passed the Senate. - .â : -'Â» >â ;
But as there is no time to read volumes
on this subject, what is here presented
may be fittingly entitled "An invitation
to thought and examination."
First, after the facts and conditions j
calling for legislation upon this subject:
From Kern, San Luis Obispo, Modoc, Las
sen and perhaps one or two of the other
counties, claims have been presented in
greater numbers than there are inhabi
tants and sheep sought to be protected
by the coyote bounty act of 1891. The to
tal of scalps claimed to have been taken ;
in the State is 57,523, which number is
found by dividing the sum for which ,
claims have been presented to the State |
Board of Examiners, namely, $287,615, by i
five ($5 being the bounty on each scalp I
under the said act of 1891). ;..â ..'
Over two-thirds of the certificates is- I
sued by the County Clerks under the said
: act are in about half a dozen hands, in
cluding banks and San Francisco law |
firms. The balance are scattered through- j
out half the counties of the State. â¢ Tnat .
the larger proportion of these "demands"
are base frauds is an admitted fact.
But the fraudulent claims are in strong I
hands, while the owners of honest claims
are in the hands of the original claim
ants, who rejected the picayune sums of
fered by the sharks and junk dealers;
and they still hold them. The latter, have
no remedy under the Senate substitute.
Now, suppose the Senate substitute
passes and is approved and a Bakers
field bank or a San Francisco law firm
files suit on a $50,000 block of County
Clerks' certificates. A trial comes on.
The Attorney General comes Into court
"to defend such suits," as provided in
section 2 of the substitute, "the holder"
offers in evidence this massive wad Of
Clerks' certificates, which, being the very
evidence prescribed bylaw, the court must
admit it, and that makes a prima facie
case against the State; and the Attorney
General is helpless. When and how is
he to obtain any competent evidence to ;
rebut or refute the case thus made? Let
there be remembered that there is at least |
$200,000 of claims so held. -â â â â -;>. .-..-
I And the next thought is as to the effect \
! upon State finances. The substitute makes
an immediate appropriation, and provides !
(section 5) as follows: "Upon presentation j
of a certified copy of such Judgment to
the State Controller the Controller shall |
draw his warrant upon the appropriation |
provided for the purpose for one-half the j
amount of such judgment payable out of
such fund for the then current fiscal year;
and thereafter, during the next ensuing
fiscal year the Controller shall In like man
ner draw his warrant for the remaining
half of such judgment, payable out of
such fund for such ensuing fiscal year."
Now, this Senate substitute, if passed
and approved, will take effect and become
a law of the State before the Ist of April.
The holders of great blocks of these certi
ficates will, in spite of anything the At
torney General can do to prevent it, ob
tain judgment and present a certified copy
thereof to the Controller during the pres
ent fiscal year, which ends on the 30th
day of June, 1899, and take out one hun
dred thousand dollars. Then on the 2d
day of July, 1899, they may demand and
collect the balance of their said Judg
ments. So under the substitute they can
within three months deplete the State
treasury to the tune of about two hun
dred thousand dollars. But not so with
the small holders, who cannot prenare for
trial in so short a time, even if they could
bear the expense, and they must sell at
whatever price the monopoly offers. In
this connection comes the suggestion in
defense of the substitute that they may
assign their demands to one another with
out litigation in their own names. Can
there be any doubt as to the class of per
sons who would busy themselves in pro
curing assignments for these purposes and
at what sacrifices to the small owners?
The substitute provides no actual notice
to any one of his right to sue the State,
nor any safeguards against an unauthor
ized appearance for claimants who have
died, removed or disappeared during the
last eight years, or against claims held in
wholly fictitious names; nor does it provide
any fund to the Attorney General for ob
taining evidence and procuring the at
tendance of witnesses for the State; nor
does it enable him to make any defense
whatever. He will be utterly powerless
in the premises; and those who are "hold
ers" of the thousands of alleged claimants
who in fact have never existed will have
it all their own way. The substitute pro
vides that the ordinary rules of practice
shall apply to such : suits against the
State, "except as herein otherwise pro
vided, with the right of appeal to either
party." The only exception therein mili
tates strongly against the small owner,
and Is in effect that he shall employ his
own attorney and pay a large bill of costs
and expenses. He would have to have
depositions taken or be compelled to. at
tend court with his witnesses time and
again in Sacramento.' Of course, the ob
stacles to obtaining any of the benefits
of , the substitute by any such persons
would be insurmountable. In short, they
are by the substitute, as it was intended
that they should be. left to the mercy of
those who have already combined and
laid their plans to acquire the balance of
the claims at their own price. â 'â¢'?-â¢,;;*

Johnson's Assembly bill 5, providing for

prevention of cruelty to animals, -was
passed as finally amended. As originally
drawn, the bill provided for the payment
of fines for offenses against humane laws
to the society causing the arrest. This
was amended to make all such fines pay
able to the school funds of the county,
and then it was amended again to bring
it back to the present form of the law,
which directs all such fines to be paid to
the society first formed in the county.
Rickard's Assembly bill 375 was passed.
The measure requires Election Commis
sioners to convene immediately after an
election and spend at least six hours a day
upon the count until it has been complet
ed This is to prevent the long-drawn-out
counts which Invariably prevent those
elected for the short terms from taking
office. '....__

THE GOVERNOR'S
VETO OVERRULED
BY SENTIMENT
Assembly Adopts the Poppy as
the State Flower Despite
Executive Objection.
CALL HEADQUARTERS, SACRA
MENTO,' March 2.â Sentiment dwelt for a
time in the Assembly chamber this morn
ing; a golden, poppy-colored emotion, that
made men for a time forget their politics
and the sordid things that men go in for
here "in this Legislature and put their
hearts and their votes into a delicate
tribute to the memory of the only daugh
ter of one of their fellow members.
It was the selection of a State flower
that drove out politics and clarified the
usual sodden atmosphere. For years there
has been a ; desire to select some native
blown blossom that would best, typify the
past, the present and the hidden future of
the State, and when â¢ this session was
younger, and cleaner Assemblyman Bliss
of Oakland offered a bill adopting the
"golden poppy" as the floral symbol of
the commonwealth.
Though he did not say so when the
measure was introduced; it was neither
more nor less than a memorial to his only
child, his daughter.
The too few years of her life she .had
spent in the wondrous garden that nature
has given' California, learning by heart
the stories and the mysteries of the blos
som.. She told them- again to the little
children of - the schools, and everywhere
she preached the "gospel of the gardens:
and Of all the multitude of posies that
?rew in them the "golden poppy" was her
avorite. To her it embodied the gold of
the hills and the gold of the sunsets, and
bore mute promise of the future. It. was
the State's natural symbol and she prayed
that the State adopt it for Its own. But
one day when the suns of sutnmer had
burned the poppies from the hills she toft
lowed them, and left only her father to
greet them when the next year came, and
urge their cause to a commonwealth not
builded on sentiment.
A glimmer of it all went through this
Legislature when Mr. Bliss offered the
bill. It. went through to the Governor with
no, question and no dissent; but there it
found neither sentiment nor a conception
of anything less gross than cold legisla
tion. Mr, Gage vetoed it and so informed
the Assembly.
' To-day his veto came up on the files,
and Grove L. Johnson told his colleagues
just why the bill was drafted.
Said he: "This is purely a matter of
sentiment. It Is neither a thing this State
needs nor a thing it needs not. It is sim
ply something it wantsâ a beautiful con
ception. It is more; it is a memorial of
fered by our loved brother. Mr. Bliss of
Alameda, to his only daughter, who died
loving the poppy, and its fairest champion.
She wanted this State to adopt the flower
and she never tired of urging it, and you
of us who have daughters will know what
this bill means and see that it shall be
come a law." -â¢** .
Assemblymen Caminetti Of Amador and
Meade of Los Angeles followed Mr: John
son, and when the veto came up to a vote
it got nearly enough against it to nullify
it. notwithstanding there were scarce af
many in the -House as were needed. But
the absentees were called, and as the
tardy ones began to arrive and when they
had been retold the story they -stood up
and voted for the flower. The Democrats
threw off political courtesy, and when th_
last count of the vote was announced
there were more than enough to over
rule the Governor's objection and send
the measure to the Senate.
Notice Of the Assembly's action was at
once sent to the Senate, and later Senator
Shortridge called it up; but politics
played a stronger hand than sentiment
In the upper house, and the Governor's
â¢veto was sustained there by a vote of 33
to 2.
SENATE SAYS NO
TAXES FOR STANFORD
UNIVERSITY
. CALL HEADQUARTERS, SACRA- j
MENTO, March- The constitutional
amendment allowing the Legislature to
exempt property of Stanford University
from taxation passed the Senate to-day,
but not without opposition or criticism.
The amendment originated in the As
sembly, and when It came to the -Senate
Morehouse and Shortridge, the two Sena
tors from Santa Clara County, stood \
ready to support it. The recommendation j
of the Committee on Judiciary, to which j
it was referred, was that the amendment |
"do not pass," but that did, not prevent j
Senator Shortridge from rising when the j
measure was reached on the file and ask
ing that it pass the Senate.
Shortridge, Morehouse and Simpson !
were all in favor of the amendment, and j
they said so to the extent of half an hour.
It was a public school, practically, was
the burden of their argument, an institu- j
tion formed for the public welfare and not '
for profit, and there was danger that un- J
less aid were granted it in the way of re
lease from taxation the welfare of the
institution would be seriously endangered
for lack of funds. It has now an Income '
of $180,000 a year, and one-fourth of this ',
is needed for taxes. The faculty costs I
$165,000, and it was remarked that be
tween the faculty and the tax there was
a deficit for ea^h year, without including
general expenses. â¢..-. r-^-; :v *â  : .
Senator Gillette opposed the amend
ment, both because of the provision al
lowing the exemption of property and be
cause of that clause which allows the
Legislature to confer, by special act, cor
porate powers upon the trustees of the
Institution. This privilege, he said, was
one which is allowed no other institution
in the State, and is directly opposed to
the principle asserted in the constitution
that ho special acts shall be passed by the
Legislature. -
â¢ Referring to the lack of funds, Senator
Gillette placed the hottest . shot that
struck the amendment. He said he had
heard that at one time there was $12,000,
--000 in taxable securities belonging to : the
Stanford estate in New York, and that it
had been moved from there and Is now
somewhere in the country producing no
revenue and taxable in this State if -the
securities could only be located. He sug
gested that two or three millions of
these securities be made to produce
some revenue and help the. university in
that way. . :. .;. â Â ' : â  : *-fi : :,'â¢-â¢ "i 'â â â 
Continuing, Senator Gillette said there
Were over 85,000 acres of land in Califor
nia belonging to the university that could
not be settled upon nor sold, and was
practically closed to the people, and he
did not think it should be exempted from
taxation. â  _ -, ,
Senator Smith did not believe in aiding
the Stanford University so long as the
State institution was so badly in aid of

dangerous experiment. He believed itwas

vest its funds in taxable property and
then exempt the property from taxation.
Stratton, Curtin and Boyce spoke in fa
vor of the amendment, if, only to allow
the people of the State to vote upon it.

CALL HEADQUARTERS, SACRA

Next to Paterson I favor W. H. L.
Barnes." : â¢; -v
> He was asked if anything would Induce

Barnes the best man. I think that this

to the front, for it seems to me to be as

WORKING TO SAVE THE p
MINERAL LAND BILL
CALL HEADQUARTERS, SACRA
MENTO, March 2.â The evening session
of the Senate was broken In upon by an
interruption from Washington. -It -was a
telegram from Marlon de Vries in which
he asked the California Legislature . to
take some action, and take it at once, in
order that he might get the mineral land
bill, of so much importance to the miners
of California, before Congress. The end
of the session was near, he wired, and
any action that was taken must be im
mediate to be of any etfect.
Senator Davis was given the telegram
and he fixed up the following- resolution:
"Resolved, By the Senate and Assemoiy
of the State of California, jointly, that we
earnestly urge immediate consideration of
the mineral land bill now pending in Con
gress, and respectfully request Hon. T.
B. Reed. Speaker of the House of Rep
resentatives, to recognize some member
of the California delegation to - call tne
same up for consideration before the close
of the present session. Â<â¢;"-. :â 
"Resolved, That the secretary of the Sen
ate be Instructed to transmit by, telegraph
a copy of the foregoing resolution to tne
Speaker of the House of Representatives
at Washington, D. C."
In submitting the resolution Davis
asked that a vote suspending the rules
he taken and that his resolution be adopt
ed and wired back to Washington- at
once. It looked as if this would be done,
but Braunhart arose and asked why the
Legislature of California should bow down
before the Czar of the House and beg him
to recognize a representative from Cali
fornia. He was answered by Davis, who
said he thought it was no time for "petty
peanut politics." and he had no 'answer
except to call for the roll call. Braunhart
explained that it was. to, the form. of the
resolution that he objected, not to the
sentiment it expressed, and so he would
Vote for it anyway, which he did. There
was not a dissenting voice and. the resolu
tion went through in a moment.
The mineral land bill now before Con
gress is one which segregates from the
land granted to the Central Pacific Rail
road Company- all lands which have been
found to contain mineral deposits, and
lays them open to settlement by miners,
giving the railroad other lands in lieu or
those taken under the act.

NO PROTECTION FOR
: '.-. TIMID CANDIDATES
CALL HEADQUARTERS, SACRA
MENTO, March 2.â The woes of the last
"conference" were thrashed over in the
Senate this afternoon' with the considera
tion of Dibble's bill to protect timid can
didates a's a basis for argument.
Dibbles bill provides that any candi
date who takes a pledge to vote for any
particular person for United States Sen
ator or any one who asked a legislator or
legislative candidate to do so. shall be
deemed guilty of a misdemeanor. There
is a law already on the books making it
an offense to exact or take a pledge, and
Dibble's bill added the question of Sena
tor to make that a sure thing. The Sen
ate had no use for- the measure, how
ever, and by a vote of 30 to 4 the bill was
defeated. -
Shortridge opposed the bill in the first
place because he said he .thought it right
and just that every one should know ex
actly how a man was going to represent
his constituents, and there was only one
way of making that sure. Davis was
also redhot after the bill. He brought m
the present situation by declaring that he
felt his people had a right to know bow
he was going to vote on the question j of
a United States Senator and on other Im
portant measures, and. he had pledged
himself on every stump in his county on
any question he understood, except those
affecting the judiciary. He thought that
if a man did not wish to pledge himself
he should assert his manhood, to- which
Senator Curtin, who favored the bill, said
"yes, and give up his ambition." .
. Curtin pointed to the. present^ condition
of affairs in the joint ballot, and declared
that If it were not for the pledges taken
before the election by the various candi
dates there would have been no deadlock
and a Senator would have been elected
long before this. "We have been here for
sixty days." he said, "and have lost an
immense amount of time balloting for a
Senator, and all because of these > ante
election pledges. How long is this going to
continue? Are we going on to witness the
disgraceful scenes we have witnessed da*,
after day in the joint session? Pledges
control all political bodies, and it has got
so now that you cannot succeed in an
election unless you have been tied down
by all kinds of pledges." Â_ââ - +Vl -
"Morehouse preferred to designate the
Senatorial bugbear as A. B. "If my con;
stttuents do not want A. B. for Senator,
he declared, "they have a right to demand
of me that I do not vote for A. 8.. and
I I have no right to violate that pledge. I
I have no "right to come here and vote for
I a man for Senator when. I know in my
! heart mv constituents are opposed to that
| man for United States Senator. I am op

GO TO THE SEAWALL
CALL HEADQUARTERS, SACRA
MENTO, March 2.â So far as the Senate
is concerned the free market will not be
on the corner of Davis and Pacific streets
in San Francisco. It- will be on section
4of the seawall. An amendment to Lang
ford's free market bill providing for the
change in the site was adopted this even
ing, despite the opposition of Cutter and
Langford, and with the assistance of Ma
jor McLaughlin, who has been persistently
lobbying against the bill in its original
form whenever he had time to spare from
Ms diminishing share in the Senatorial
fight. Senator Stratton proposed the
amendment moving the site for the addi
tion to the free market from the lot lying
between Pacific street, Davis and the
water front to section 4 of the seawall.
Dickinson spoke first on the amendment,
saying the site originally mentioned was
occupied by the Pacific Coast Steamship
Company and was paying the State a
rental of $600 a month throughout the
year, and by the bill it was intended to
oust the company to make way for a â 
traffic which would pay but for three or
four months.
Cutter read several resolutions passed
by the Supervisors, the Chamber or Com- |
merce, the State Grange and the Fruit
Growers' convention, all of which recom
mended the site on Davis and Pacific I
streets. Btratton explained that improve- ;
ments valued at $50,900 would have to be j
removed or given up by the company, and
showed that the site on the seawall was
Just as good and quite as close to the cen- ;
ter of trade so far as transportation was
concerned as the one the original bill se
lected.
Others spoke on the question princi- [
pal";.- on the subject of the railway facil- !
ities, which Senator Langford explained j
â¢was what the farmers most wanted, and j
then the amendment went to a vote. It j
had but little to spare when the vote was
â¢announced J but the amendment had car
ried and the bill was laid over until it
could be printed.

I herewith return to your honorable body,

The language of the bill la so obscure, where
It purports to permit full satisfaction to be ;
entered after a sale under foreclosure, that It
would, apparently, release any Judgment
debtor from a deficiency Judgment after fore- i
ensure. HENRY T. GAGE.

bill 113 and reads as follows:
This hill amends section 1050 of the Code of
Civil Procedure by adding thereto the follow
ing words: "And In such action any defense I
may he made by plaintiff to such claim which j
he "would be entitled to make were an action i
brought by the defendant against the plaintiff." ,
If the language of this amendment Is to bear .
the most favorable construction, then It adds '
nothing to the strength of the original sec- j
tlon, for this has been the -settled law upon I
the subject.

CALM BEFORE THE STORM.
Chinese Say the Trouble Is Over, but
Police Look for Shooting.
"No more trouble" is the watchword
! of the Chinamen now, and In consequence
! of this voluntary information that the war
I is over the police are on the alert for more
! shooting, for past experience has taught
I them that when everything is quiet and
! there are no threats of violence some
: thing will happen.
The war between the SI On Sear and
the Bing On tongs is reported to have
I been satisfactorily settled. The Sam _ups
' held a meeting yesterday at which rep- I
: resentatives from both tongs were pres
' net. After considerable powwow it was :
1 agreed that the relatives of Chan Si ,
- Wing who was murdered on Jackson i
; street Saturday night, should be paid $500
and each of the three tongs to which !
the dead man belonged should receive $250. i
, It is nrobable that after this money set- !
i tlement is made Wong Yum Hung, who
is under arrest for the* murder, will not |
j be prosecuted by the company. Â---'_
Chin Goey. who threatened the life of j
his cousin. Chin Gin, Monday night and i
was released on bail after being arrested,
I made other threats yesterday and was |
rearrested. The meeting of the Chin fam
ily held Wednesday night did not result
in an adjustment of the trouble and Chin.)
Gin and his brother stated to the police j
last night that they feared they would
be shot by some of the Hop Sings, to |
j which Chin Goey belongs. Neither of the i
i brothers will venture on the street with- j
! out a white body guard.
THE CATHEDRAL MISSION.
Interesting History of the Rise of the
Charitable Institution.
The year book of the Cathedral Mission
of the Good Samaritan, just issued, gives
an interesting history of the mission from
its foundation and a record of the work
done in the past year.
The mission was first opened on May
4, 1894, at 249 Second street, its object
being the entertainment of men and boys
in the neighborhood. It consisted of but
five rooms, and was looked alter by a
delegation from the Brotherhood of St.
Andrew. ,
It was a success from the start, and a
year after its opening a subscription was
started to raise funds for the erection of
a new building. The sum of $Â«.Â«>â¢> was
raised, and on December 27, ISM, the cor
ner-stone was laid for the chapel and mis
sion house. Later the adjoining lot was
purchased, and the corner building, con
taining sixty-five rooms, was leased and
fitted up for the working boys. The mis
sion was crowned with success from the
start, but the question of finance has ever
been a vexing one. About $5000 will be
needed to carry on the work during the
coming year, and it is hoped to raise this
by subscription.
A STRANGE SUICIDE.
Meiner Duer Hangs Himself in a Va
cant House on San Bruno Road.
The body of Meiner Duer. salesman for
William Hoelscher & Co., liquor dealers
at the corner of Taylor and Turk streets,
was found hanging in a vacant house at
I'lavbank wharf on the San Bruno road
early Wednesday evening. The firm s de
livery wagon, which was driven by Duer.
was found tied to a fence near by. The
body was taken in charge by the San Ma
teo County officials and an inquest will be
held to-day. â¢ _ v _ ,~ . , , I
Wednesday morning, about 10 o clock,
Duer drove away from the- liquor store
with a consignment of liquors which were
to be delivered to different parts of the
city. At that time he said nothing that
might be construed as an intention to
commit suicide and he was in apparently
good spirits. He had been in the employ
of the firm for the past ten months and.
so far as is known, his accounts are all
r Duer resided at 2105 Howard street. He
leaves a widow and two young children.
Previous to his employment by "William
Hoelscher & Co. he was a driver for the
United States Brewery.

Had Been Under Treatment at a Hos
pital for a Week.
James McDonald of Boca, Nevada
County, died at St. Mary's Hospital yes
terday afternoon. He came to this city
about a week ago and entered the hos
pital to undergo treatment for affections
of the heart and kidneys. He improved
slightly for a few days, then gradually
sank until yesterday, when he breathed
Mr. McDonald's loss will be felt keenly
by sportsmen all over the State. There
was good shooting and fishing on his .
grounds in Nevada County and all sport- j
ing men were made welcome and treated ;
royally. â â ';â ', _ .
He was also well known in business
â¢circles from ins connection with the Boca ;

leaves three sons and one daughter. The
remains were taken to the undertaking
parlors of Carew & English on Van Ness j

WELL KNOWN AUTHOR GONE.
Theodore Kirchoff Died Last Night
After Two Weeks' nines*.
Theodore Kirchoff. an author well
known in this country and in Germany,
died at his residence, 809 Scott street, last
Mr. Kirchoff was taken sick about two
weeks ago with inflammation of the lungs
and has been steaaily sinking. He was
a native of Germany, and his best literary
works were in the language of his na
tive land, although many of them have
been translated.
Newspaper Stamps for Sale.
The Postofflce Department announces
that beginning on Saturday morning at
8 o'clock and every day thereafter phila
telists, or stamp collectors, may have an
opportunity of buying newspaper and
periodical stamps in unbroken sets at $5
each.
There are only 50,000 sets, and when they
shall have passed into private hands they
will be greatly Increased in value. The
denominations are from 1 cent to $100 each.
They will be sold at the stamp window at
the general postofflce. ." .-:.
Library at Manila.
Lieutenant Colonel J. D. Miley, who
leaves for Manila on Saturday, has com
pleted the preliminary work on this side
for founding a free library for officers
and soldiers at Manila. He has arranged
with W. S. Jones, president of the Se
curity Savings Bank, to receive contribu
tions for the purchase of books. The
Call will set aside a bundle of papers each
day which will be forwarded to Manila
as "the opportunity offers.
Used a Dirk on His Wife.
Jacob J. Pfrang, a piano player, was
yesterday held by acting Police Judge
Barry to answer before the Superior
Court on a charge of assault to murder
in $1000 bonds. On February 18, he and
his wife had a quarrel in a saloon at
723 Howard street, when she knocked
him down, and he stabbed her three times
In the neck and shoulder with a dirk
knife.
Drunken Parents Arrested.
Officers Tracy, Lynch and Delmar of the
Eureka Society made a raid last night on
the house at 430_. Clementina street, oc
cupied by Michael Cum, his wife, mother
in-law and three children, the latter aged
7 years. 18 months and 1 month, respect
ively. The officers found Mrs. Cum and
her mother lying drunk on the floor with
the younger children beside. them. Cum
was sober enough to get out of the house
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, FRIDAY, MARCH 3, 1899.
when the officers entered, and took the
oldest child, a boy, with him. The chil
dren will probably be sent to a charitable

cared for.
MORMON LEADER HERE.
President Joseph F. Smith. Regrets
the Utah Legislative
Scandal.
President Joseph F. Smith, leader of
the Mormon sect in Zion, Utah, who has
been for some time in Honolulu on busi
ness connected with his church, has ar
rived in this city and is staying at the
Grand Hotel. With him are Mrs. Smith
and his two nieces.
"I regret that my visit to the islands."
said Mr. Smith, "compelled me to be ab
sent at the very time the Senatorial fight
was going on in Utah. I deeply deplore
the scandal that has arisen in the case.
No matter whether the charges of brib
ery against A. W. McCune are proven or
not, McCune cannot go to the Senate un
tainted. Although I consider him a good
friend of the Mormon church, still we
hardly care to assume the responsibility
of his election.
"As to Congressman-elect Roberts, I
honor him for his fidelity to his covenants
and his devotion to his church, but I think
his election at this time Is a mistake.
However, the people wanted him, and
they are responsible and not the church."
President Smith addressed the Mormon <
Mission at 915 Golden Gate avenue last
night, and the Mormon colony was well
represented. He spoke of matters re
lating to their faith and urged them to be
true to it.
President Smith will probably leave for
his home shortly, as he received a tele
gram yesterday that the investigating
committee in the McCune bribery case
expected to bring in their report to-day.

â¢
Mrs. Birmingham's First Public Ap
pearance Since Her Return
From Europe.
Mrs. Birmingham gave a song recital at
the Sherman & Clay Hall last evening,
making her first public appearance in San
Francisco since her return from Europe,
where she has been studying singing for
nearly three years with Bouhy and Hen
schel. She has a contralto voice of ex
ceptional power and quality and of large
range. With these gifts and her evident
intelligent and handsome appearance,
Mrs. Birmingham is sure to meet with
success as a concert singer If she contin
ue- in her profession. She sang a dozen
songs last evening, finding her, best op
portunities in the "Air de Dalila." ..from
Salnt-Saens' "Samson and Dalila. the
four Schubert songs and Henschel s fine
song, 'There Was an Ancient King.
Mrs. Birmingham Was assisted by Mr.
Weiss 'cellist, and Mrs. Batchelder, ac
companist. _ '
Appealed, to the Wrong Court.
A communication was received by the
Supervisors yesterday from a coursing
man with a grievance. The letter was
from W. W. Harrington, who claimed
that he purchased two greyhounds in
February. paying $125 for the brace of
dogs. After the purchase he found that
they had been ruled off the local fields
before coming into his possession. He
asked the Supervisors to reinstate them.
The hounds are George Dixon and May
McKinley. The communication was placed
on file.
Owner Wanted for a Wheel.
Captain Gillen of the Seventeenth street
police station has in his keeping a Victor
bicycle for which he wants an owner. The
wheel was left in the yard of Henry-
George at the southwest corner of Treat
avenue and Twenty-fifth street one week
ago during the night. The number on the
wheel has been defaced. The police are
tinder the impression that the machine
has been stolen and was left there by the
thief.
Sharpers Rob a Farmer.
Henry Hansen, a farmer of San Mateo
County, swore to a complaint last night
at the California street police station for
the arrest of Fisherman Jack, a noto
rious character of the Barbary Coast.
Hansen claims that he was robbed by
Flsherman Jack and two of the latter's
pals In the Monte Carlo saloon, on the
corner of Jackson and Kearny streets,
last Sunday afternoon.
Refuses to Prosecute.
When the case of John Toomey, the
young tough who threw a lighted lamp
at his mother at 333 Harriet street on
Wednesday night, was called In Judge
Mogan's court yesterday Mrs. Toomey did
not appear, and the Judge continued the
case and issued a bench warrant for her
arrest. Toomev has already served six
months in jail for beating her.
Fractured His Leg.
W. H. Howell, an employe in the quar
termaster's department at the Presidio,
had charge of a lot of Government mules
at Sixth and Berry streets yesterday,
when one of them broke away from the
herd Howell started in pursuit, and his
horse slipped and fell. His left leg. was
fractured and he was taken to the Re
ceiving Hospital for treatment.

ONE VOLUNTEER
SPEAKS HARSHLY
OF CANNED BEEF
_-_ ww. rrr _â¢ â  â  V_7._ll _-_ _^
But His Testimony, as Well as
That of Other Witnesses,
Is Contradicted.
WASHINGTON, March There
j were several interesting developments
'â¢ in the beef court of inquiry to-day. It
j was a field day for the volunteer arm
of the service, the waiting room being
filled all day with members of the
various volunteer regiments which
served in Cuba and Porto Rico.
The first of these, Clarence Walters,
of the First Volunteer Engineers, told
a sensational story of hardship and in
humanity suffered during his service,
describing the canned roast beef in
harsher terms than have ever before
been applied to that much discussed
article.
Later In the day, Lieutenant Sewell.
U. S. A., who was major of the volun
teer battalion in which Walters served,
contradicted his story completely, say
ing there was no canned roast beef
aboard the transport which took the
command to Porto Rico. Further than
that, he said Walters had come to him
after landing and asked to be dis
charged, so as to accept a position as
newspaper correspondent, and on being
questioned, said that he had been well
treated while in the service and had
no complaint to make.

REFUSED TO CONSIDER
GENERAL WHEELER'S CASE
Significant Political Division in the
House on the Report of the
Judiciary Committee.
WASHINGTON, March 2.â An attempt
was made in the House to-day to con
sider the resolutions reported by the Ju- j
diciary Committee, declaring that General
Wheeler and the three other members |
who accepted commissions In the army j
had thereby vacated their seats, but the |
House by an overwhelming vote refused I
to consider them. The political division
upon this vote was significant. The vote
stood 77 ayes cast by twenty-one Repub- j
licans, forty-three Democrats and fifteen
Populists; and 148 noes, cast by 101 Re- j
publicans, forty-four Democrats and one i
Populist.
A half million dollars each was appro
priated under suspension of the rules for
the Pan-American Exposition at Buffalo
and the Ohio Centennial at Toledo.
The Senate bill, carrying $1,000,000 for a ;
new building for the Department of Jus
tice, was passed,, and two public build
ings in other cities were agreed to. one j
appropriating $250,000 at Los Angeles, Cal.
A number of conference reports on I
minor bills were adopted.

An important development was the
denial of the story by one James Far
nan, and also attributed to Dr. Maxwell
Christine of Philadelphia, that they
had seen beef injected with chemicals
in an Omaha packing house. Dr.
Christine explained that what he had
seen was the pickling of corned meats,
the brine being injected into the meat
to save several weeks' time in the pick
ling vat. _, ,
The question of counsel for General
Miles before the court is still in abey
ance, some correspondence having
passed between the general and court,
but both sides decline to give out the
letters until a settlement is reached.
General Miles has not asked to be
represented by counsel, presumably be
cause that would put him in the posi
tion of admitting himself on trial by
the court, which up to date he has de
clined to admit. On the other hand
the court does not feel able, under the
articles of war, to permit Major Lee
or any one else to cross-question wit
nesses unless he can be recognized offi
cially as counsel for the defense. It
was stated that this matter probably
will be settled to-morrow. The court
expects to start West Tuesday or
Wednesday of next week.
DIRECTORS ARE
YET RESIGNING
Three More of the Union
Bank Retire.
FOUR NEW MEMBERS CHOSEN
PRESSURE OF STOCKHOLDERS IS
BROUGHT TO BEAR.
One More Place on the Board Is De
manded, and a Knotty Prob
lem Is Referred to the
Attorney General.
4
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SAN JOSE, March Three more di
rectors of the Union Savings Bank re
signed to-day under the pressure
brought by stockholders. They were
Thomas Rea, H. C. Morrell and E. A.
Wheeler. One vacancy already ex
isted, and L. Calisch, Asa Eaton, J. W.
Rea and Cyrus Jones were elected
members of the directory. These are
men whom the stockholders selected
for places yesterday.
President W. H. Wright and N. Bow
den are the only members of the old
board remaining. C. P. Brestan of San
Francisco is the other rr. ember and was
elected a few d.ays ago.
The directors had agreed to make F.
W. Potter one of the board as a repre
sentative of the depositors; but some
hitch occurred as to whether the stock
holders' power could be transferred to
him under the bank's condition, and
the matter was referred to the Attor
ney General for an opinion. * This will
come up at the meeting to-morrow
afternoon.
Some one will have to resign to make
room for Potter. The four directors
elected to-day declare they will not
make room for the depositors' repre
sentative, and some speculation is be
ing indulged in as to which of the two
old ones will step down and out.
NOT IN FAVOR OF
THE EXPOSITION
Assemblyman Feliz Writes to His
Monterey Constituents in Reply
to the Trustees' Resolution.
MONTEREY, March 2.â A letter from
Assemblyman F. P. Feliz to the Board of
City Trustees of Monterey in response to
t^ie resolutions passed by that body re
questing Monterey's representatives in the
State Legislature to vote for a State ap
propriation for an exposition in San Fran
cisco in 1901 has Just been received and is
as follows:
SACRAMENTO, Feb. 23. 1599.
To the Honorable Board of City Trustees of
the City of Monterey, â Gentlemen: A copy
of your resolution of the 17th Inst. ; relative to
"Pacific Ocean International Exposition" has
been duly received. While your action receives
Its fullest appreciation and consideration, and
carries with It no small weight of Influence.
permit me to say that I have given this mat
ter much consideration and study and my con
viction* at this time stand boldly against the
proposition. I am open to conviction in the
matter, however. If your honorable body can
formulate any chain of reason which shall con
vince me I am In the wrong I shall be pleased
to listen to the same. Very truly,
F. P. FELIZ.
No further action was taken upon the
subject by the Trustees, but the board
and Indeed the town as a whole strongly
indorse the exposition.

KIPLING ON THE
WAY TO RECOVERY
Condition Continues to
Improve.
COMFORTABLE THOUGH WEAK
SEES NEWSPAPERS AND LIKES
INTEREST SHOWN.
Now There Is Some Concern Over the
Condition of the Patient's Chil
dren, One of Whom Has
Pneumonia.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
NEW YORK, March 2.â That Rud- |
yard Kipling'- condition has imnroved j
very greatly during the past twenty- j
four hours Is evidenced by the fact that
but one bulletin was posted during the
day. That was of a very satisfactory
nature, and it was announced to-nis-ht
that no further bulletins would be forth- j
coming until to-morrow afternoon. !
The bulletin issued at the Hotel Gren- i
oble at 3 o'clock this afternoon was:
"Mr. Kipling has made satisfactory !
progress. He has a slight fever, but ;
is comfortable though weak, and reso- j
lution is taking place in the affected
portion of the lungs.
"E. G. JANE WAY.
"THEODORE DUNHAM."
Another indication that the patient
is getting along well is that to-nleht he >
asked to be shown the newspapers and
seemed to be greatly pleased and aa- t
fected at the interest in his illness j
shown by the American people.
Mr. Kipling spent a comfortable
night. He slept for some hours and j
rested well, and again this morning he
enjoyed an hour or two of sleep. In j
this way he has in a great measure
recuperated his strength, and the
promises are very bright for his speedy |
recovery.
According to the latest reports Kip
ling's two little daughters are not seri
ously 111, as was at first thought. The !
elder, Josephine, a child of six, is be- j
ing cared for at the home of Miss |
Deforest, a friend of Mr. Kipling; and >
to-night Miss Deforest said that the I
little one was doing "very nicely, in
deed." This was after Dr. Janeway
had visited the child, with whom he
spent a considerable time. She Is suf
fering from pneumonia, and it is not
known whether she is absolutely out
of danger. The younger of Mr. Kip
ling's daughters, Elsie, a child of three,
was said to-night by Mr. Doubleday
to be suffering from nothing more seri
ous than a severe attack of bronchitis,
and it is hoped that the physicians will
be able to save her from pneumonia.
At 8:30 to-night Mr. Doubleday came
down from the Kipling apartments :
and said that the patient was making ;
rapid improvement. - V V.
"Dr. Janeway will not come back to ;
the hotel to-night," said Mr. Double- |
day. "There is no reason why he i
should. Mr. Kipling is making such
progress that Dr. Janeway's continued
presence is unnecessary."
INVESTIGATION OF THE
SHOOTING OF KISHMOTO
Yolo County Officers Insist That
Japanese Accuse Whites in Or
der to Shield Themselves.
"WOODLAND. March Attorney R. E.
H. Epsey and H. Fumikura, a representa
tive of the Japanese Consul at San Fran- j
Cisco, returned to this city from Blacks j
Wednesday evening, where they went to |
make an investigation of the shooting
of the Jap named Kishmoto. Upon their
return Epsey stated that the matter had
been investigated thoroughly and he is
convinced that the story that two white
men did the shooting is true.
In details the particulars gained by the
recent investigation differ' to some extent
from the account published at the time
of the shooting. According to the later
story it seems that Kishmoto heard a
noise outside the cabin and got out of bed
to investigate. He saw nothing and re
turned. Ho was followed into the cabin
by two white men. who, either because
they thought he would resist or for the
purpose of intimidation, shot him, the j
bullet taking effect in the shoulder. This j
aroused all the Japs and they were hud
dled together and one man covered them
with a pistol while the other searched
their personal effects. This job being
over the two men left the cabin and
quickly disappeared in the darkness.'
This last Investigation was probably in
stituted with a view of making the de- ,
velopments a basis for a claim for dam
ages. The county officers and Mr. How- :
ard still adhere to the theory that the |
shooting was the result of a row among :
the Japs and that the robbery story is a
pure invention to shield one of their num

TERRIBLY BURNED BY
A GASOLINE STOVE
LOS ANGELES. March 2.â M. Jones,
a well-known business man of this city,
was horribly burned about the face and
head this morning while in the act of
filling a lighted gasoline stove. That he
was not burned to death and his home de
stroyed by fire was due. to the heroism of
Mrs. Jones and her mother, Mrs. Van
Sickles.
Mrs. Jones was aroused by a piercing
shriek, and, rushing into the kitchen,
found her husband's clothes enveloped In
flames. She secured a blanket and threw
it over him, and Mrs. Van Sickles seized
a pail of water and helped extinguish the
flames. _____
Jones was horribly burned about the
face and neck, and his condition is pre
carious. A strange feature of the accident
is that Jones- is a dealer in oils.
WATER ALIVE WITH SARDINES.
Run of the Shining â  Mites in Mon-
terey Bay.
MONTEREY, March 2.â An extraordi
nary run of sardines has existed in Mon
terey Bay for the past two days, especi
ally in the immediate vicinity of Mon
terey. The shiny mites are here in such
enormous quantities that the water ap
pears literally alive with them in num
bers of spots of several feet in diameter,
and fishermen say they can be dipped up
without difficulty with hand nets, Large
shipments are being made to the various
California markets. This run of sardines
presages a like good riyt of salmon, whose
natural prey the sardines are, and sports
men as well as the professional fishermen
hereabouts are anticipating a fine busi
ness before the close of the week.

JAPAN SEIZES
ARMS SHIPPED
TO AGUINALDO
Serious Blow to the Cause
of the Rebels.
Special cable to The Call and the New York Herald. Copyright, 1899,
by James Gordon Bennett.
5 MANILA, March 3.â It : is reported here that a steamer +
Â° with 20,000 stands of arms and ammunition, which were Â°
+ bound for the Philippines, has been seized in Japanese waters +
â¦ by the Japanese authorities. +
MANILA, March 2.â Aguinaldo's
army is disheartened as the re
sult of recent actions. Many
companies of the insurgents
have retired from the field
because their officers sulked, and
would not lead. their men.
One hundred Filipinos ran forward
toward the American lines one day re
cently, shouting: "We don't want any
more fight. Viva los Americanos."
The local press comments on Gen
eral rtios' failure to obtain the liberty
of the Spanish prisoners. Aguinaldo
refuses to liberate the priests unless
they give up their landed estates, and
he also imposes the condition that the
Pope shall recognize the full rights of
the native clergy who, because of their
rebellious attitude toward their su
periors are liable to excommunication.
The latest news from Cebu is good.
The natives are returning to the town
and coolies are now willing to work at
reasonable wages. Under the native
government they asked double pay.
Vessels are loading ana business has
been resumed.
Influential natives of the islands of
Malate and Ticao request General Otis
to send troops there. They say that
200 men could easily subdue the rebels.
The inhabitants are peaceable and are
disgusted with the extortions of three
successive native governors.
The two islands contain 80.000 head
of cattle, which constitute the food
supply of the rebel army of the south.
This is the hottest day of , the season,
but fortunately all is quiet inside and
outside the American lines, and the
majority of the men are kept in the
shade.
The United States transport Morgan
City has arrived here. The wives of
the officers and other women passen
gers were not allowed to land, the au
thorities considering the conditions
ashore too unsettled.
The outposts beyond San Pedro Ma
cate were fired upon by the rebels from
the walls of the Guadalupe church.
A gunboat advanced 300 yards beyond
the lines and cleared away the enemy
with Catlings. No casualties are re
ported. ,
General Miller has . been ordered to
quarter his troops in lloilo in the cus
tom house or other public buildings
and authorized to make the necessary
changes at a cost not to exceed $40,000.
the same to be met from the public
revenues collected in lloilo.

WHY GERMANS ARE LEFT
TO AMERICAN PROTECTION
BERLIN, March 2.â A high German
authority, who prefers to be nameless,
assures the correspondent here of the
Associated Press that the motives
which induced Germany to intrust the
protection of Germans in the Philip
pine Islands are simple. The Kaiserin
Augusta and other German warships in
the Far East are needed in Chinese
waters, owing to the recent disturb
ances, and Germany feels that German
Interests are safe under, the protection
of Americans. This implies, of course,
a high compliment to the United States,
but it is only in consonance with sev
eral previous declarations made by
Baron yon Bulow, the German Minister
of Foreign Affairs.
Regarding Samoa, the same high Ger
man authority said Germany was
awaiting the initiative of the other
powers. If the division of the islands
is to be carried out the officials of the
German Navy Department say the Ger
man third-class cruiser Falke will be
relieved by the German fourth-class
cruiser Sperber. The new German gun
boat Yagur is going to China, making
a total of twenty-five German war
ships on foreign service.

With them replacing the same number

the effect of a sudden and forced dis

of the peace treaty.
DEWEY WANTS THE
SMALLER GUNBOATS
WASHINGTON, March 2.â Admiral
Dewey is again pressing on the Navy
Department the urgent need of vessels
of light draft as a means of communi
cation between the small islands of the
Philippine group. , To this end the gun
boat Vixen, which figured bravely for
j a vessel of her light caliber at the out
| set of the Cuban campaign, has been
; ordered to be immediately fitted out at
I the Norfolk navy yard for sailing for
Manila. This will take about a month,
i and it probably will be three or four
| months before she joins Dewey's fleet-
I Besides the Concord, which is perhaps
I too large to be classified as one of the
I small craft, the only light vessel now in
! the Philippines available for running
I about in those waters are the Petrel
\ and the Helena. The Castine and the
Princeton are now speeding in that di
rection, but the small craft equipment
I there will still be inadequate. The de
j partment is anxious to afford Admiral
; Dewey all the assistance possible. He
; has repeatedly called attention to the
great importance of this work and has
I emphasized the fact that it is light ves
sels, fit to skirmish about in the shal
low waters and to run in and out of
I harbors where the larger ones would
be useless, that he wants rather than
i the heavier types. ;'.i

TOWNSEND MAY BE
MINISTER TO SPAIN
NEW YORK, March 2.â The Wash
ington correspondent of the Herald tel
egraphs: Believing Spain will soon rat
ify the peace treaty, President McKin
ley is considering the appointment of a
Minister to Spain. Information ob
tained to-day indicates that the ap
pointment will probably be given to

Portugal. He has had considerable dip
lomatic experience, having been secre

ister to Spain until after the ratifica

in Washington.

INSANE PATIENTS
TO WORK A FARM
Innovation at Stockton for Which the
Legislature May Grant an
Appropriation.
STOCKTON, March What kind of
farmers will the patients of an insane
asylum make? That is the question which
arises with the announcement that a
truck farm is to be operated in connec
tion with the big asylum at this place.
The board of managers has reason to ex
pect favorable action this week by the
Legislature on its request for an appro
priation with which to purchase land to
undertake this novel venture. It will be
an innovation which will be closely
watched by other institutions of the kind
throughout the land and by those who
wish to see State institutions put as near
1 as possible upon a self-sustaining oasis.
The tract of land it is proposed to pur
i chase is on. the San Joaquin River, five
; miles from the asylum. It is ferine and
we'll adapted in every way for truck farm
â¢ ing. There are about 100 acres in the
i farm. It was impossible to procure land
: adjoining the asylum property, as it is
'â  all within the city limits, and town lots |
, would be rather expensive even for a ricn
State to experiment upon.
The working force to the number of ,
about 100 a day is to be conveyed to the
I farm every morning and returned in good
! season to the asylum, and eventually,
' when the North-street canal is opened to
I the river, the farm may be reached by the
I water route, as the canal runs along the
\ northern boundary of the asylum grounds
There are about 300 men of young and
medium age, physical strength and light ;
mental maladies who will be available for
the work, Experience around the grounds
â  has demonstrated that considerable work
can be got out of the patients as in
nearly every instance they are daft on
' Snly one or two subjects. They are
i whimsical about their work, hut what the
'â  overseer lacks in quality he will have in
quantity. Many of the patients are pos-
I sessed of a distinct aversion to work and
i of enough cunning to refuse to labor on
the ground that they are Patients and.
I cannot be forced to do so. There is said
to be some legal merit in this contention
but diplomacy is all-effective in dealing
\ with this class of unfortunates Y\ her i a
i strike seemed to be imminent the induce
ment of extra tobacco was held out as an
l incentive for industry among those af
flicted with laziness. Tobacco generally
; had the desired effect, and the 'kickers
: would resume their spasmodic efforts at
The" idea of taking 100 crazy men through
the city and working them in the open is
not relished by those who reside near the
farm, or even in this city; but the board
of managers will probably surround them
j with such safeguards as to obviate any
excuse for a public protest. The under
taking is at best an experiment, and no
one except a few of the asylum directors
seems anticipate that the farm will
reduce the asylum expenses to any notice
able degree. The only benefit will be in
supplying work for patients. The physi
cians, as a result of experience and ob
! servation, have found that work has been
beneficial in many cases that have come
tinder their care. But the experiment
from a financial point of view, is at least
I worth watching.
THE PLANET JUPITER'S
ERRATIC MOVEMENTS
Professor George W. Hough Thinks
He Has Made an Important
Discovery.
CHICAGO, March 2.â Professor George
W Hough, the astronomer at the Dear
born Observatory in Evanston, has made
public a new discovery in regard to the
surface rotations of the planet Jupiter,
which is completely at variance with the
existing belief. It is the general tenet of
astronomers, and the statements are em
bodied In the most recent textbooks on
the science, that the surface rotation of
the planet Jupiter is similar to that of
the sun, being quickest near the equator
and slowest in the high latitudes near the
P ln his conclusions, based on a long rec
ord of observations extending over
twenty years, and which he has just com
pleted, Professor Hough announces his
belief that the surface of the planet Ju
piter rotates upon its axis in separate en
velopes or strata.
Professor Hough further discovered In
the long series of observations that the
spots shift- in longitude, besides having
a rotary movement, and this also sup
ports the ring or strata theory, strength
ening Professor Hough's belief of many
years that Jupiter is in a gaseous or plas
tic state. -
SOUTHERN CALIFORNIA
INDIANS DESTITUTE
Driven Back by Civilization, Many
Are Now Sick and on the
Verge of Starvation.
LOS ANGELES, March. 2.â A.
Wright, chief Indian Agent for the South
ern California Indians., says that the In
dians of Southern California are in desti
tute circumstances.
"Something must be done, that is all,"
declares Mr. Wright. "The Indians have
never had such a year before. They
need clothes and food, and many are sick,
some having consumption. Once they had
the best in the land. Now they have the
worst. They are driven to the rocks and
mountains. They have hitherto con
trived to live on beans and acorns, but
civilization has encroached upon them
and unless it rains very soon their little
garden truck will amount to nothifig, and
they cannot find work. There has not
been a case of smallpox among the In
dians of Southern California."

els and ex-Senator Felton left for Phila

eral days, then proceed to New York and
thence to San Francisco.

LEO DID NOT
CONDEMN REAL
AMERICANISM
View Taken in Rome of the
Letter to Cardinal
Gibbons.
DUST CLEARED AWAY
Archbishop Ireland Rightly Inter
preted the Views of the
Sovereign Pontiff.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
ROME, March 2.-â The im
pression prevails here, now that
the dust has settled and things
can be plainly seen, that the
Pope's letter to Cardinal Gibbons
approves real "Americanism"
and condemns those who had
misrepresented it. The Pope col
lected, examined and definitely
repudiated all such misrepresen
tations.
Incidentally he believes that
the things so alleged never ex
isted in America, for the Ameri
can Bishops would have de
nounced them, and his Holiness
thus vindicates true "American
ism."
Archbishop Ireland's letter ap
pears to have emphasized the
Pope's views, and it is generally
understood that Cardinal Ram
polla, Papal Secretary of. State,
has assured him that the Pope
never condemned and never in
tended to condemn real "Ameri
canism," declaring also that
Archbishop Ireland had rightly
interpreted the views of the Pope,
and urged him to make this fact
known.
NICARAGUA INSURGENTS
ARE NOW DISPERSING
Cowed by the Joint Action of the
United States and England in

COLON, Colombia, March 2.â The United

SUICIDE OF A YOUNG WOMAN.
While Delirious Prom Fever She
Jumps Into a Well.
BLACKS STATION, Cal., March 2.â
Miss Phille Kotterman, a young woman
20 years or age. residing with her parents
five miles west of this place, committed
suicide at 10 o'clock to-day by jumping
into an open well containing ten feet of
water. For the past few days she had
been suffering with fever, and while in a
delirious condition escaped from her
mother and committed the act.
