COULDN'T SEARCH
FOR PROF. ARDREE
Return of Noted French
Aeronauts.
CONDITIONS NOT FAVORABLE
DR. TERWAYNE CONTEMPLATES
A NEW VENTURE.
With His Associates He Will At
tempt to Reproduce Dawson
City at the Paris Ex
position.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
VICTORIA, B. C, March 2.â Among
the recent arrivals in this city from
Dawson are three of more than ordi
nary interest. They are Count E. de
Rouloux, M. P. E. de Vick and Dr.
Terwayne, a party, of noted Parisian
aeronauts and scientists, who went
north about a year ago, having an
idea of attempting an aerial voyage
into the polar regions in search of An
dree. They left the balloon in Van
couver (it was afterwards sold to the
United States for war purposes and
was used during the unpleasantness
with Spain), and went north to famil
iarize themselves with the conditions
existing in the Yukon Valley.
To The Call correspondent Dr. Ter
wayne explained that he very soon
abandoned the idea he had formulated,
first because of the difficulty of obtain
ing the necessary chemicals for the
manufacture of the inflating gas, and
principally because the climatic con
ditions were distinctly unfavorable for
aerial navigation. An ascent would be
impossible at Skaguay because of the
prevalence of contrary winds, and in
the passes it would be too dangerous
for similar reasons. Further north th?
entire absence of wind prevented any
possibility of success, and so the doctor
was compelled to reluctantly abandon
his cherished enterprise.
Arriving at Skaguay on his return
journey Dr. Terwayne received the-in
telligence of Andree's remains having
been found, and he congratulated him
self and companions upon the fact
that they had not braved the same
fate upon what would have proved a
hopeless quest.
Dr. Terwayne is nothing if not orig
inal. He has conceived a scheme dar
ing in its originality and magnificent
in its possibilities. It is nothing more
nor less than an imitation of the Klon
dike in Paris, and the doctor is has
tening as fast as steam can take him
to the French metropolis to arrange, if
possible, for the necessary space
either in the exposition grounds or ad
jacent thereto.
Interested in the scheme are some of
the millionaires of Dawson, and money
is the least momentous consideration
to the enthusiastic and vivacious
Frenchman. The plan is to construct a
replica of Dawson's main streetâ log
cabins, dance halls and allâ people it
with genuine Klondikers. and also to
show an actual representation of the
mining operations as carried on along
the creeks. There would be so much
realism about It that gold dust would
be scattered about and be washed up
In orthodox fashion, and as far as pos
sible the scenery of the Klondike cap
ital would be duplicated.
"Of course," said Dr. Terwayne. "it
Is uncertain. It is possible though,
and if I can get the ground I shall
show the Parisians what Dawson Is
like." i --M
"You can tell the readers of The
Call," continued the doctor, "that there
will be an epidemic in Dawson this year;
they care for nothing but money, and
all the filth is allowed to remain on the
streets is horrible. And I can also
tell you that men in Dawson are not
earning the money they were. Many
will work for little more than a living,
but there is little work doing, and I was
paying the highest wages to my party
of any employer, 85 cents per hour. The
royalty is made the excuse for suspend
ing work on many claims, the men
thinking it will be taken off.
"I can give you some news that will
Interest Californians," said the doctor.
"The Berry Brothers, who hail from
there,. were in danger of losing their
rich claims on El Dorado. The claims
are very valuable and they were taking
out over $150,000 a season. The lease
lapsed some time ago and by a mis
take in the date they failed to renew it
until one day after the. time allowed..
In the meantime an intimate friend
who knew the workings of the claim
stepped in and jumped it. The brothers
protested, but the Dawson courts held
that the jumping was quite legal and
the owners had to pay $40,000 to get
their property back. 'â [:;.

ALTON RAILROAD
SOLO TO COULD
Through Trains From
Sea to Sea.
MEANING OF THE DEAL
VANDERBILTS INTERESTED IN
THE SCHEME. .
There Will Be Trouble in Store
Now for All of the
Great Transcontinental
Roads.
Sr-ctal Dispatch to The Call.
' CHICAGO, March 2.â John J. Mitch
ell, president of the Illinois Trust and
Savings Bank, upon receiving word
from New York that a majority of the
stock of the Chicago and Alton had
been deposited with the United States
Trust Company, and that pledges had
been given for the deposit of many
more shares, gave out to-day the mem
bers of the purchasing syndicate. They
are George Gould, president of the
Missouri Pacific; Jacob Schiff. chair
man of the board of ' the Baltimore'
and Ohio; James Stillman. president
of the National City Bank of New
York, representing the Rockefeller
holdings in the Missouri, Kansas and
Texas, and E. H. Harriman. chairman
of the board of the Union Pacific, rep
resenting the Vanderbilt interests.
Harriman is also understood to rep
resent to some extent the Illinois Cen
tral road, which is indirectly interested
in the deal. Beyond the expenditure
of $3,000,000 for the improvement of the
property generally, and another $2,000,
--000 which is to be set aside for the work
of elevating the tracks of the company
at Chicago, it is understood that the
syndicate has not yet made any defi
nite plans for the future management
of the road. Mr. Blackstone will prob
ably retire from the presidency, and
Vice President and General Manager
Chappell may succeed him. Mr. Black
stone was offered the chairmanship of
the executive committee, but declined
It. The other officials of the company
will, it is understood, retain their pres
ent positions. v." .
Chief among the men who owned and
have sold the road were Marshall Field,
A. C. Bartlett, A. A. Sprague and John
J. Mitchell.
The price paid, counting in the bond
ed debt, is $74,505,676.
With the tidings of the consumma
tion of the deal came the report that
the new owners of the Alton would sell
its share in the Union Depot Company,
owning the Union Depot terminals at
Adams street, and would become les
sees of the Chicago Railway Terminal
Company, with its passenger station at
Harrison street and Fifth avenue.
The purchaser of the Alton's share
in the Union depot, according to the
report, will be the Pennsylvania. The
latter road wants this terminal for its
Panhandle line. . ,7V ;;;:.. 1
The sale of the Alton will, it is said,
have a far-reaching effect on the rail
road business of the entire country.
Transcontinental trains running from
tidewater to tidewater Without change
and without breaking up, even at Chi
cago or St. Louis, are now a probabil
ity. , . â¢ .-â ^â  :
ATTEMPT TO AMALGAMATE
WAS NOT SUCCESSFUL
General Conference of the National
Reform arty Concludes Its
-r _.
Labors.
CINCINNATI, March The general
conference of the National Reform party
concluded Its work to-day at noon. Its
organization preparatory to nominating
an independent Presidential ticket next
year will be completed when its national
executive committee meets at Buffalo
June 29. The effort to amalgamate the
Populists, Silver Republican. Socialist
Labor party, the National Liberty party
and other scattering elements was not
successful. There were only 154 delegates
present, representing fifteen States. Joel
Parker of the Texas Press Reform Asso
ciation and Dr. H. A. Thompson, a lead
ing prohibitionist, and other leading re
formers who participated yesterday, were
not present to-day and the advocates of
amalgamation freely expressed their dis
appointment over the results of the gen
eral conference, i &

LABRADOR LOST
ON SCOTCH COAST
All the Passengers Reach
the Shore.
AGONCILLO AMONG THEM
BEHAVES LIKE A COWARD
WHEN SHE STRIKES.
It Is Probable That the Vessel and
Her Cargo Will Prove
to Be a Total
Loss. / â
Special Dispatch to The Call.
TOBERMORY, Scotland, March 2.â
The Dominion line steamship Labrador,
from St. John, N. 8., for Liverpool, ran
ashore about four miles from Skerry
vore on Wednesday morning. Captain
Erskine, her commander, has sent the
following dispatch to the agents of the
steamship at Liverpool:
"Labrador ran on Mackenzie Rock in
a thick fog at 7 o'clock yesterday morn
ing, four miles from Skerryvore. The
ship was caught amidships, the seas
breaking over. Holds Nos. 1, 2 and 3
were soon full of water. The passen
gers and crew were saved in the boats.
One boatload was landed at Skerry
| lighthouse. The German steamship
| Viking picked up the remainder. No
I steamship is obtainable to return for
! the passengers. If the ship is not broken
up will endeavor to save the mails
when a steamship Is obtained."
The Labrador had a good passage un
til Sunday, when she entered a fog
bank and lost her bearings.' Skerry
vore light was mistaken for Inistrahull
light, on the north of Ireland. Most of
the passengers were still in bed when
the steamship struck and there was
much excitement, but the passengers
were calmed by the assurances of the
captain, the boats were lowered with
i out a hitch and all on board left the
steamship in safety. _â ;''. >-'_
The passengers and crew lost every
thing they possessed. The women and
children were put in the first boat and
the other passengers in the. second
boat. The crew of the Labrador took
the other boats. > â¢â¢â -;â  -.
After three and a half hours the boats
met the steamship Viking from Nor
way, which took them on board.
W hen the Labrador was last seen she
was settling down by the stern. The
passengers of the wrecked steamship
are full of praise for the Labrador's
officers and crew and for the conduct
of the captain of the Viking.
Agoncillo, the Filipino representative,
who ran away from the United States
on the outbreak of hostilities at Ma
nila, was a passenger on the Labrador,
and behaved like an arrant coward
when the vessel struck.
OBAN, March 2.â half dozen of the
rescued passengers of the Labrador
have arrived here. All speak of the
suddenness and completeness of the
disaster. One lady was obliged to leave
jewelry worth $3000 behind her, and an
other "passenger, a man, left $1500 in
cash.
The lighthouse steamer left Oban this
afternoon to try to take off the passen
. gers imprisoned in . the - Skerryvore
lighthouse and to salvage the mails.
CAUGHT AT SACRAMENTO.
Close of the Tour of Two Bold Bicycle
Thieves.
SACRAMENTO, March Frank Ster
ling and Charles T. Randell are behind the
bars here for stealing a 'bicycle. They
were among a gang who deserted from
Mare Island on February 19 and set out
from San Francisco on bicycle stealing
tours. They stole two wheels from a cy
clery near Goiden Gate Park and sold
them in San Jose. They stole two wheels
in Santa Cruz and sold them in Watson
ville, returned to San Francisco, rented
two wheels and pawned them. In Oak
land they stole two more and then came
to Sacramento. They stole a wheel here
yesterday and were arrested to-day. Ster
ling made the confession.

San Francisco, CaLNO AGREEMENT ON
LEGISLATION FOR
NICARAGUA CANAL
Senate and House Conferees
Struggling With River and
Harbor Bill.
WASHINGTON. March 2.â The river and harbor bill conferees ad
journed shortly after 10 o'clock to-night until tomorrow morning at 10,
having been unable to agree. Chairman Frye of the Senate conferees said:
"We have been unable to agree, so that is all there Is to say."
He was asked, "Is there any prospect of agreement?"
"I can't say," he replied. "it looks very dubious."
The conferees discussed the different propositions and counter proposi
tions which have been made regarding the Nicaragua canal bill, but upon
adjournment to-night the House conferees as a whole refuse to allow any
declaration for a Nicaragua canal in the bill although Burton is willing
to have a; declaration of policy and liberal appropriation for a commis
sion to select the best rout The Senate conferees offered a proposition
looking to an investigation of the best route, leaving the selection to a
mission, but insisting that provision should be made for beginning
work. Between the confer. there is still a wide difference and no mem
ber of the conference will predict an adjustment to-morrow.
Some of the big questions in the bill have been settled; The Senate pro
visions for the harbors at New York. Philadelphia and Baltimore will be
retained. There is a sharp fight over the reservoirs at the waters of
the Missouri River, the indications being that the Senate will yield. It was
also asserted that the House conferees refused to accept the Senate amend
ments for Improvements in Oregon, known as the Yaqulna Bay and Colum
bia River boat railway projects, and that they would have to be compro
mised.
The conference committee or. the river and harbor bill has agreed to the
appropriation for Aiviso harbor. This is gratifying to Congressman Loud,
who has been earnestly working to secure this item.

many that American so-called southern fruits, like oranges, lemons and
raisins, are not subject to the provisions of the decree of February 2.
IS9S. providing for the examination of fruit in order to determine whether
they are infected with the San Jose scale.
HURLED OVER
AN EMBANKMENT
Returning Soldiers in a
Train Wreck.
|
i
FIFTEEN ARE BADLY INJURED
'i
i
i
THAT NONE MEET DEATH IS j
CONSIDERED MIRACULOUS.
i
i
j
; Missouri Troopers Who Had . Just j
Been Mustered Out Overtaken
by Disaster While Sound
Homeward.
'
EpedaJ Dispatch to The Call.
TUPELO. Miss., March 2.â The Mo
bile and Ohio train bearing homeward
the second battalion of the Second
Missouri Volunteers from Albany. Ga~.
where they were yesterday mustered
out of the service, was wrecked two
miles south of this place this morning.
Four cars in which the soldiers were
seated jumped the track and were
hurled down a twenty-foot embank
ment. The third car from the engine
was a total wreck, and from its ap
pearance the escapes were miraculous.
The following were injured:
Jake Stanley, Company E. Pierce
City. Mo.; Jack McLaughlin, Company
E, Monet, Mo., hurt In back: Thomas
A. Greathouse, Company K. Rockvllle.
Mo., hand, shoulder and leg injured:
C. C. Drake. Company G. Warsaw,
Mo., left side; W. B. Edmonson. Com
pany F. Calhoun. Mo., gash in the
head; Peter Wolf, Company F. back;
L. W. Stlckrod, Lewis Station. Mo.,
hand split and gash in leg; F. W.
Waldon, Company H. back: William
Morris. Company H. Nevada. Mo.,
scalp wound; E. G. Kelsey, Company
G. seriously; H. G. Helm, Company 'Â
Schell City, Mo., right arm broken:
H. W. Ryan. Company G. Camp
Branch, Mo., cut on the head: Otis
Nicholson, Com-jany G, Edwards. Mo.,
back and hip injured; A. T. Ready.
Company F, Nevada, Mo., internal in
juries; W. L. Rudisill, Company F.
Nevada, Mo., back; George East. Com
pany A, Clinton, Mo., internal injuries.
The injured were immediately taken
from their perilous position by their
more fortunate comrades, who worked
bravely.
The position of the cars was ex
tremely dangerous after the first
crash, as they hung over the brink of
a trestle that crossed a stream twenty
feet below. Fortunately they held their
position, for a number of fatalities
would have occurred had the sleeper
gone down. The physicians of Verona
and this place worked faithfully dress
ing the wounds of the injured, and the
citizens have rendered all tne assist
ance In their power.
The rear truck of the tender jumped
the track just as the train reached the
trestle and tore it away. The baggage
car, two day coach and one sleeper
went off the track and down th" em
bankment. All the men who are able
to travel left here this evening and
should reach St. Louis to-morrow
morning.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, FRIDAT, MARCH 3, 1899.
TWO PROMOTIONS
ARE POSTPONED
Nominations of Schley
and Sampson.
SENATE TAKES NO ACTION
FORTIFICATIONS MEASURE IS
DISPOSED OF.
Passage of the House Bill to Encour
age the Holding of a Pan-
American Exposition at
Buffalo.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
WASHINGTON. March 2.â The Sen
ate in executive session to-day took up
the Sampson and Schley promotions
and discussed them briefly. Senator
Butler moved to consider the nomina
tion of Admiral Schley, Independ
ently of the nominations. but
it being apparent that no Quor
um was present, the Senate went
into open session. The motion remains
pending and will be pressed by Senator
Butler at the next executive session.
The greater part of the session to-day
was devoted to unobjected nomina
tions.
In open session, pending considera
tion of the fortification bill, the follow
ing measures were passed: Authoriz
ing the Commissioner of the Freed
men's Trust and Savings Company to
pay 62 per cent dividends to depositors:
a House bill to encourage the holding
of a pan-American exposition on the
Niagara frontier at Buffalo. N. V.. in
1901.
The Senate then proceeded with the
fortifications bill, the bill being in pre
cisely the same form as it passed the
House. It carries $4,744,79-.
Pettigrew proposed an amendment
striking out an appropriation of $65,000
for the purchase of a Gathmann gun
and one of $10,000 for the ammunition.
The amendment was defeated.
The Senate, on motion of Hoar, at
6 o'clock, went into executive session.
At the conclusion of the executive ses
sion, the Senate resumed legislative
business at 6:15 p. m.
After remarks by Foraker on the
death of Representative Stephen A.
Northway of Ohio, the usual resolu
tions of regret were adopted, and the
Senate took a recess until 8:15 p. m.
When the Senate reconvened Quay
moved that the Senate concur in the
amendment of the House to the meas
ure providing for the erection 'of a
building for the Department of Justice,
the amendment limiting the cost to
$1,000,000. The motion was agreed to,
which passed the bill.
An effort was made by Senator Fair
banks to obtain consideration of the
bill to extend the anti-contract labor
laws of the United States to Hawaii,
but Morgan, one of the Hawaiian com
missioners, objected and the measure
went over. 'â¢.,
Consideration of the fortifications bill
; was resumed and Tillman was recog
-1 nized, to reply to speeches made earlier
In the day by Gorman and Lodge. He
said that it was an anomalous condition
, that the conference on the naval bill
was in control of men who were op
\ posed to the Senate amendment to re
duce the price of armor plate. He was
; willing and anxious to give the admin
; istration all the support it needed, both
for the army and the navy.
Later Piatt of Connecticut Inquired
I If Tillman believed the Krupp armor
. could be procured at even $445 per ton.
Tillman replied that perhaps it could
â  not be, but he was convinced that the
extra price for Krupp armor was "sim
| ply a scheme to rob us out of $145 a
ton. ' He was willing to complete every
battleship now on the stocks and pay
$400 a ton for the armor, but he wanted
Congress to proceed to the erection of
an armor factory and thus free the
i country from the clutches of the armor
â  trust.
Piatt of Connecticut said he desired
' simply that the war vessels of the
United States should be covered with
the very best armor made, and he
thought all other Americans felt as he
did. He did not believe that England,
Russia and Germany were submitting
to the exactions of an unconscionable
j trust and it was in evidence that they
j were paying about $575 a ton for the
J Krupp armor.
: Chandler of New Hampshire declared
j In answer Â«to questions that there was
j no evidence that the Krupp armor was
! any better than the Harveyized armor,
j as no Investigation had been made of
armor since Secretary Herbert made
his Investigation. N
The fortifications bill was then passed
without division. The bill was passed
precisely as it came from the House,
without amendment, and goes to the
President.
The conference reports on the census
and naval personnel bills were agreed
to and these measures also go to the
President.
WILL NOT RACE IN THE EAST
Burns & Waterhouse Weary of Play
ing a Losing Game.
NEW YORK, March 2.â A private letter
from San Francisco to a racing official
here says Burns & Waterhouse will prob
ably not race in this part of the country
this year. The letter goes on to say the
firm wants to race in New York. but its
members realize they must have "sure
enough" race horses to get money here,
and they are shy on that commodity.
They have nothing really high cla-*s on
which to depend, their best one being on
the ailing or stale list now.
Previous attempts to campaign here
have been expensive, and they nave grown
tired of the outpouring of dollars with but
small chance of getting any of It back.
Burns & Waterhouse are not entering in
any of the Eastern stakes now closing.
SWING OUT
THE JAPANESE
SLAVE TRAFFIC
The Trial of Toda, the
Procurer.
BOTHER WITH INTERPRETERS
|
SOME REMARKABLE ANTICS OF j
THE DEFENSE.
|
I
A Despicable Business That Is Car- I
ried on Openly Under the Eyes
of the Federal Port
Officials.
Toda, the Japanese charged with keep- :
i ing a minor girl in a house of ill repute, i
: gave his testimony in Judge Groezinger's '
i court yesterday afternoon. As a statement
maker Toda is peculiar. He began by in- i
: slstlng in excellent English that he could j
; not speak that tongue and demanded an
: interpreter. The court had been taxed to ;
I the limit of its patience by Japanese in- |
terpreters, and the demand was not re- ,
[ ceived with good grace.
Toda a few months ago was the prose
cuting witness in a suit against two of
his countrymen, and his knowledge of the
English tongue was at that time clear
enough to win bis case, but after some
skirmishing among the large audience of
Japs an interpreter was caught and
sworn. Then Toda started in to ruin the
cause of the State. The girl Chiyo had
stated on the stand that Toda had given
her about $10 and her mother or some
relative the same sum in Japan when he
engaged her to come to this country for
employment in his. handkerchief store,
and that when she arrived In San Fran
cisco, an ignorant immigrant girl, 16 years
old. she learned for the first time her
hideous fate. In the hands of the ruthless
slave dealer. Toda. she was helpless, and
when she failed to earn a reasonable
amount of money he beat her. To make
her condition more pitiable, she ' was
forced to entertain his Chinese friends
and to hand her owner every cent given
her. __****-_ "Â«â¢â¢
The Japanese slave dealers In this city
charge their chattels $300 for bringing
them over from Japan, and under the pre
tense of letting the girls work themselves
out of debt allow them a small percent
age of their earnings. But they manage
to keep that $300 standing against their
bondswomen by inflicting fines whenever
it suits their fancy. : â - : â .-.â â 'â¢
Toda swore that he never saw the girl
in Japan and never offered her employ
ment in a store anywhere. He also swore
that the place at 3 Brooklyn place
did not belong to him. and that he was
only the cook there. He couid not read
his'own name. _"ââ¢_,
The rent and grocery bills of .-, Brooklyn
place are all mad" out In his name, yet he
denied the ownership. He also denied
that the woman with whom he lives and
who is evidently the mistress of his es
tablishment is his wife, though he has
called her such since his arrest.
Attorney Donavon, for the defense, ap
peared to be trying to get the case over
into Japan. He claimed if the girl Chiyo
was inveigled into a San Francisco house
the inveigling was done in Japan, and the
State of California was usurping a func
tion of the Mikado in prosecuting Toda.
Court Interpreter <*-effeney. though not
on duty, was present as a sort of assist
ant for the defense, consulting in whis
pers with Donavon. His action drew
forth a strong protest from Attorney
Reardon of the prosecution.
At the close of Toda's testimony the
trial was continued until next-Monday af
ternoon. _ , _.'â¢'"â â .-- 'â â --_ i_

sible the importation of Japanese girls

place who are engaged in the traffic un
der the eves of the Federal port officials,

ble business in The Call has caused Jap
anese Consul General Mutsu to place his

A BLOODTHIRSTY BAKER.
Carlo Ferro Arrested for Threatening
to Wipe Out a Whole Family.
Carlo Ferro, a baker, is of a blood
thirsty nature and threatened to wipe out ,
a whole family within twenty-four hours.
Ho appeared before Judge Graham yester
day to answer a charge of threats against
life
The complaining witness was Mrs. Lucy
Gulnasso' 11 Barman place. She testified t
that last Tuesday Ferro called at her
he.use and threatened to kill her and her
children within twenty-four hours unless
she paid him $300 which he alleged her late
husband had owed him. She was afraid
that be would carry out his threat.
Ferro claims that there is $300 owing j
him from the Gulnasso estate, of which j
the widow is administratrix. The time i
expired for all accounts to be sent in j
against the estate unknown to him. and i
when he demanded the money from Mrs.
Gulnasso she refused to give it to him. !
The case was continued till to-morrow.

Tried a Sharp Game.
William Deal, a respectably dressed .
man, went into Tillmann & Bendel's store
Wednesday " afternoon and represented
that he was a relative of John F. Deal,
a prominent grocer In Santa Barbara. He
asked them to advance him $5. and they '
could telegraph to Santa Barbara. He
got the money, and when one of the j
clerks began to fill in a telegraph blank
be ran out of the store. He was chased !
and captured and held until Policeman
Handley made his appearance. Yesterday
he appeared before Judge Graham on a
charge of obtaining money by false pre
tenses and the case was continued.

IMPURE WATER,
HOP TOBACCO
AND PEA COFFEE
How the Crew of the
Phelps Fared.
OVERDUE SCHOONER'S LUCK
BARK TEMUCO TO DISCHARGE
AND REPAIR HERE.
The Dauntless Breaks Down at Her
Wharfâ Arrival of the Overdue
M. P. â The Cey
lon's Damages.
The long overdue schooner W. S. Phelps
finally made port yesterday. Kor nearly
two- months the crew has been fighting
head winds and calms, and when th? ves
sel's anchor was dropped off Meiggs
wharf every man on board heaved a sigh
of relief. For six weeks not one of them
had washed his face, and until the
schooner put into Santa Cruz they had
been smoking hops for tobacco and mak
ing coffee out of ground split peas. Kor
eight days they had been without meat,
and their drinking water was foul. The
decks leaked .and in the forecastle the
crew bad to sleep in wet bedclothes and
walk through puddles of water until near
ly a.. o: them have rheumatism.
"We left San' Pedro for Eureka fifty
one days ago,' said the mate yesterday.
"I have been through some tough times
on the Atlantic and in the Arctic, but
such a voyage as the one we had on the
Phelps I never heard the equal of. The
vessel had been lying a long time in San
Pedro before she was sold by the Mar
shal, and during that time she got very
dirty. She had, previously to her arrival
in San Pedro, lost her centerboard, so
you see she was not In the best of trim
for a voyage. Then our water was no
good, but that fact I did not discover
until we were out it sea. It was so of
fensive we could hardly drink it. We
landed at one of the islands in the Santa
Barbara Channel and got some fresh
water, or I do not know what we would
have done.
"Well, if you are anything of a sailor
you can imagine .what kind of progress
we made without a centerboard and bar
nacles two feet thick on her bottom. We
were three weeks In rounding Point Sur,
and then we made fair progress. Fre
quently, however, we would make a run
of twenty-four miles during the day ami
drift back twice the distance during the
night.
â¢'On Sunday. February 19, we were off
Santa Cruz, and the captain determined
to run in for supplies. At that time we
were back to the foul water, our coffee
had given out and we were using a con
coction made from split peas. We had no
canned meats, sugar or tea. and, worst
of all. no tobacco, so we had to smoke
hops as a makeshift.
"We sailed from Santa Cruz as soon as
we got some supplies, and on Tuesday.
February 21. we got past Pigeon Point
and were standing inshore with a west
erly wind, as Captain Alexander of the
Santa Rosa reported us. That night,
however, the wind took a change and the
next morning we had been driven back
forty-two miles south of Pigeon Point.
That was the kind of experience we had
right along, and our twelve days' run
from Santa Cruz to San Francisco was
one that I hope never to duplicate."
The Phelps will go on the dry dock here
and will have a new centerboard put
in and her decks recalked. after which
she will proceed to Eureka to load lumber
for San Pedro.
The Chilean bark Temuco. which put In
here in distress last Wednesday night,
will probably discharge her cargo and go
on the drydock for repairs. She was
bound for Victoria, B. Â«'.. but was caught
in a gale on February 25 and lost her
jlbboom, fore topgallant mast, fore top
mast, main royal mast and main top
gallant mast. Besides this, she was very
much strained and will require a thor
ough overhauling.
The Temuco was formerly the American
bark Independence and was built at Bos
ton in 1871. Several years ago she was
.old by C. P. Hemanway of Boston to
M. B. Peede of Valparaiso, and was put
under the Chilean flag. She is 165.6 feet
long, 34 feet 2 inches broad and 22 feet
7 Inches deep. She has been in the ni
trate trad-' ever since her purchase by
Mr. Peede.
The Union Transportation Company of
Stockton is having a run of hard luck.
Its last steamer broke down yesterday
morning, and until repairs can be made
the company's freight and passengers
will have to be carried on the opposition
steamers. Last Tuesday night the Cap
tain Weber broke her shaft when be
tween the Brothers and Pinole Point.
Her passengers were taken to Stockton
on the opposition steamer Mary Garratt
and next day the Dauntless towed her
to San Francisco. Yesterday the Daunt
less arrived from Stockton, and when
docking at Clay street wharf her shaft
broke, so both steamers are helpless. It
will take about a month to repair the
vessels, and in the meantime the steam
ers of the California Navigation and Im
provement Company will carry all the
freight and passengers. â¢
The surveyors went over the bark Cey
lon yesterday and Inspected the damage
she received while in collision with the
British ship Talus. The captain of the
Ceylon asserts that the Talus drifted

The Schooner W. S. Phelps and the Chilean Bark
Temuco.
The Phelps was fifty-one days coming here from San Pedro and the Temuco was partially dismasted
on February 25. The crew of the schooner had a terrible time getting here, and the bark will have to dis
charge her cargo and go on the ways for an overhauling. She was bound from Iquique for Victoria, B. C.
and put in here in distress. .

The transport Portland got away for
Manila yesterday afternoon. She car
ried a large cargo of supplies, besides a
number of wagons and ambulances for
General Otis. pome of the Midlers who
were to have gone away on her escaped j
from the wharf as soon as they were
paid, in order to get a drink, and were
left behind. The Portland was signaled
off Lime Point and came to a stop, while
the men piled Into Peterson's launch
Amy and were put aboard after an hour's
delay.
The American ship M. P. Grace arrived
front, Sydney. Australia, yesterday after
an unusually long passage of 102 days.
Her copper sheathing Is loose, and in the
light winds and calms encountered it
practically anchored the ship. The Brit
ish ship Brussels is now out ninety-nine
days from Newcastle, Australia, and 8
per cent reinsurance Is being paid on
her.

v THE GOLDEN POPPY.
CALIFORNIA may rejoice. The poppy, gleam
ing yellow on a thousand hillsâ perhaps on even
more, to say nothing of the peaceful valleys
betweenâ has been vindicated. No executive veto
was sufficient to dim its splendor, nor did its
gorgeous petals shrivel under the blast of official dis
approval. The Assembly says that the poppy shall
be the State flower.
If there was one thing California particularly needed
it was a State flower. At times there has been a
yearning for rain, for a rise in the price of prunes, for
a loftier statesmanship, amendments of the code, a
giant exposition, an end to Burns. However, there
has been on all these subjects a lack of unanimity.
Much as there might be to commend them, their ad
vocacy has been certain to provoke opposition. Not
sc with the elevation of the poppy. With one ac
cord the public heart swelled big at the' thought and
the public voice, clamoring for fruition of the plan,
was is the roar of many seas.
Strangely enough, Governor Gage did not perceive
the purport of the tumult. He may have mistaken
it for the sound of wind escaping from a Senatorial
boom. Thus this act, so benign, so fraught with des
tiny, the finished product of cultivated minds, ran
against a veto. It was not to be crushed. It gath
ered itself up, took a run and jump and sailed to vic
tory so high that it can turn back and say to the veto,
"Never touched me."
Scotland has its thistle, England its rose, Ireland its
shamrock, and now California springs from obscur
ity and, wreathed in bloom as yellow as a Hearst
daily, joins the procession. We congratulate every
body, the commonwealth which now realizes that its
Legislature did not meet in vain, and the poppy it
self. Once the great of a people were decorated with
laurel and with bay. That was long ago. Times have
changed. Laurel and bay aren't in it now. The poppy
holds the belt.
In â book called "Yesterdays in the Philippines"
the writer says: "The American national policy and
the American military system. do not fit in with the
administration of 1400 islands,. 8000 miles away from
the American mainland, sweltering in the tropics,
swept by typhoons and shaken by earthquakes and
harboring 8.000.000, more than three-quarters wild
colored people, speaking a score of languages." This
densely ignorant author evidently never heard of the
Journal-Examiner? "national policy," which is big
ger than any typhoon or earthquake, and peculiarly
adapted to savages.
One woman is accused of having recently caused
the death of four of her own sex by criminal opera
tions. While the facts are for the courts to determine,
there is a marked absence of any reason for believing
her innocent, and the present would seem to be an
excellent time to express disapproval. Where are the
health officials? That woman is equal to a malady.
The Montana Legislature has passed over the veto
of the Governor a bill to permit the big stockholders
of a corporation to freeze the little ones out. There
has been something queer about that Legislature
from the first, but of course a Californian would feel
a delicacy in saying so while the one at Sacramento
remains on exhibition.
If Cubans get gay and order the United States to
withdraw they will merely be setting far into the fu
ture the day when the stable government promised
them might be theirs. And they may set it so' far as
never to be able to catch up with it. . ; ;; "â 'â "â¢.
Matrimony Promoter Bain is now employed in a
jute mill across the bay. People who depend upon
him to provide a partner of their joys and sorrows
will be obliged to remain single for fifteen months.
If the question of holding the Philippines were left
to the men who have done and are doing the actual
fighting in those islands, it would be quickly settled
in the negative by a vote probably of 08 per cent.
One day the correspondents have the Kaiser mak
ing faces at Uncle Sam and the next giving him the
glad hand. These correspondents ought to get to
gether and adopt a platform.
Dewey declines to state why he desires the pres
ence of the Oregon, and Creelman refuses to en
lighten the world on the subject. At least half of the
situation is surprising.
Carlyle said "that man everywhere is the born
enemy of lies." But he had not visited Sacramento
when Dan Burns was a candidate for the Senate.
NOW IS THE TIME TO STOND.
THE sixty day limit of the Legislature has ex
pired. From that expiration to the end of the
session the members serve without per diem.
In the situation which early developed in the Burns'
campaign it is evident that his hopes for success were
projected to this very time. Personal reasons indis
pose many members to longer desert pressing private
business, and they desire to get excused to go home.
Under ordinary circumstances many such leaves of
absence might be sought and granted without harm.
But now the case is^ifferent. The future of the party
j appeals to the patient loyalty of Republicans and the
| welfare of the State is a consideration affecting Demo
j crats. Every day's experience, since the Senatorial
I fight began has made it clearer that the vast majority
of the people of the State are opposed to making
Burns Senator. His fight has lost popular strength
from the beginning. Those who were disposed to
tolerate it as one of the inevitable incidents of politics
have come to regard it with active aversion. Indif
ference has been turned into a keenly sensitive feeling
that the State cannot afford to turn away from the
spirit that is engendered in its universities and schools
and by its many agencies of morality and refinement
and select such a man for Senator. The State is en
j titled to the service of its best men, its most thought
i ful, and by seeking such service it constantly advances
i its position and encourages young men to hope for
J recognition as a reward for trained intelligence and
| scrupulous fidelity to public trusts. What California
! father can encourage his son in the path of culture
and manliness, in expectation that the common
; wealth may recognize his quality and command his
! service, when the State's highest prize in public life
| has been given to a man like Burns!
Now is the time of trial, and now is the time to
j stand. Let every member stay at his post. The
! Burns men will, as a part of their tactics, readily vote
jto excuse any man whom they cannot control. They
! will be glad to reduce each House to a bare quorum.
| If they can get a joint convention reduced to sixty
-1 two members, a quorum of each House, they enter-
I tain a desperate hope of offering such inducements
!as to secure the thirty- two votes necessary to elect as
I a majority of such a convention. But they have no
i hope of securing enough to make a majority of a full
I convention. In this situation every member who
I seeks a leave of absence and gets it brings Burns
! nearer to the success of his scheme, and cannot evade
j the responsibility of his action.
The cry of the State to her legislators, therefore, is
|to stand to their posts and their duty in these last
j days. It is plain now that if this is done a Senator
can be elected who will be no discredit to the party
or the State, and the future of Republicanism in Cali
fornia can be made safe.
. This is a crisis for the party and the State. If
Burns succeed, when will party leadership be raised
| out of degradation? How many years must pass be
| fore men who have the right to aspire to such a
; high office will be encouraged to appear? How long
j a time must elapse before a Republican can vindicate
the honor of his party before the people? By
j standing firmly now and maintaining the high ideals
> in which it has won, the party will vindicate itself
! and make its future sure by its record alone.
I But, if it fail now, to whom can it look for a vin
j dication? Will Burns undertake to go upon the
i stump next campaign and secure party success by the
display of his intellectual gifts? Will any of his sup
porters perform this office? Three men came to him
after the first ballot. If he is successful they will
naturally have eminence and leadership. Will the
party listen to Wright, Jilson and Simpson? Will it
accept them as examples to follow and samples of its
best intellect and. morality? If Burns win, the party
will have no, choice in the matter. These three will
be held up to the people as the representatives of its
best, and there will be no escape.
The State looks to every man in Sacramento who
j represents its best purposes to do his duty and
stand.
MR. HUNTINGTON'S PROMISE.
COLLIS P. HUNTINGTON has written to
Edward Ivison of Santa Barbara a letter con
cerning the long delay, of the Southern Pacific
Company in closing the gap in the coast road be
tween Surf and Ellwood, the right of way for which
was given by the residents along the route years ago.
As the letter is characteristic of the writer and relates
to a matter of public importance it merits more than
a passing notice.
Mr. Huntington began with the statement: "I am
glad to be able to say something encouraging in the
matter in which you and other residents of your sec
tion are so much interested." He then went on to
say: "We have succeeded in getting Mrs. Stanford
and Mr. George Crocker (who are now in New
York) to agree to go on and close this gap between
Surf and Eilwood as soon as it well can be, at least to
work* up to the economical point, and I hope the
trains will be running through your city in about
twelve months after we get fairly to work."
It appears from this that the delay in the work and
the failure to keep faith with the property-owners
who gave the right of way on the understanding that
the line across the gap was to be completed at once
have been due not to Mr. Huntington, president of
the road, but to his wicked and dilatory partners.
These have at last been won over, and Mr. Hunting
ton now says the encouraging thingâ ,is that
the road will be running through Santa Barbara
within twelve months after the company gets fairly to
work. "v - â -"; ;-.V, -". :
The amount of encouragement in the statement de
pends upon the answer to the question when the com
pany will begin work and get fairly started. A con
siderable number of years have passed since assur
ances were first given to the people between Eilwood
and Surf that the road would be completed speedily
when the company got to work. For all those years,
however, the company has carefully abstained from
getting to work, further than was required to take
possession of the right of way and hold on to it.
Mr. Huntington has just given $1000 to "the Mer
cantile Library fund, and for that he deserves and
will- receive credit and praise. It is a generous deed
well worthy of emulation. It will be seen, however,
that his promise as regards the closing of the gap
has to it a string strong enough to bind the leviathan
or haul a freight train. Since a promise of that kind
was first given the promoters of the Valley road have
constructed a railway from Stockton to Bakersfield
and have advanced far toward completing the con
nection between Stockton and this city. Still the
short gap between Eilwood and Surf remains un
closed. The promise has now become venerable for
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, FRIDAY, MARCH 3, 1899.
j its age, but the amount of encouragement in it has
lessened with the years. The people are waiting to
I hear when the company intends to get fairly to work.
SUPREME COURT CONGESTION.
SEVERAL plans for the relief of the Supreme
Court have been placed before the Legislature
and are receiving attentive consideration. For
years that tribunal has been congested to a degree
that has seriously obstructed the administration of
justice in this State. It has had the aid oi a competent
and industrious commission, latterly "of five mem
bers, and yet the congestion is unrelieved, although
to some extent reduced. When seven Justices, with
five assistants, are unable to cope with the appeals
taken in a State with a million and a half of inhabi
tants there must be something wrong with the sys
tem. Upon this point all lawyers and citizens gen
erally will agree. There is no complaint of the court
itself. It is always in session, and the work done by
its members is heavy and thorough. Each case de
mands and receives full consideration, and cannot be
slighted because of the accumulation of records and
the consequent delay. There can be no adequate re
lief without the establishment of an additional court
or additional courts, and this necessarily involves
amendments to the constitution that must satisfy the
public or they will be beaten.
There are rooted evils in the entire judicial system
of the State as administered which affect. the business
of the Supreme Court and require drastic treatment.
The real points on any- Ordinary appeal were for
merly . presented with precision and terseness in a
few pages of print. Now, with rare exceptions, they
have to be dug "out by the Judges from literal trans
cripts of reporters' notes, swelled to absurd propor
tions through professional' indolence or incom
petency at the expense of litigants. It often happens
that the transcriptions of shorthand notes, without
discrimination, and the printing bills are the largest
items in the cost of a lawsuit, and thus the heaviest
work is improperly transferred from the offices of the
lawyers to the chambers of the Judges. But this
class of interferences with justice requires separate
treatment. The immediate remedy, imperatively de
manded by t v s. exigencies of the situation, is a clean
division of labor, so that the Supreme Court may not
be buried under the conglomerated mass of jurisdic
tion by which it is now oppressed.
One of the plans submitted to the Legislature pro
poses the reduction of the number of Supreme Court
Justices to five and the establishment of three inter
mediate courts of appeal, each with three Judges,
and with authority in the Legislature to provide for
additional courts of appeal as the business of the
State may warrant. In many cases the decisions of
these appellate tribunals would be final, but the direct
appeals to the Supreme Court and the instances in
which cases would find their way there from the in
termediate courts of appeal would still be so numer
ous that it is doubtful whether the system would be
an improvement. The main difficulty with this plan.
however, is its cost, liable to be frequently increased
through the exercise of legislative discretion/which
almost certainly would insure its defeat, at the polls.
A more practical and economical scheme,, the sim
plicity and apparent effectiveness of which will prob
ably receive the approbation of the bar and the
bench and also attract the favorable consideration of
the people, is the organization of a new court of ap
peals, to consist of three Justices, to which a large
part of the appellate jurisdiction is to be finally and
exclusively transferred. . It is designed that this j
tribunal shall have sole charge of such appeals as j
may be taken in criminal cases, on questions of law
alone, in actions not based upon though some of them
may originate in contracts and, as the lawyers say,,
sounding in tort, which would include claims for un- ;
liquidated damages, in divorce and alimony suits, and
in "'special cases," such as forcible entry or detainer, -
insolvency proceedings where not superseded by the
national bankruptcy law, suits to prevent or to abate
nuisances, contested election cases and cases of quo
warranto to determine the legal title to public offices.
This transfer of jurisdiction would not throw upon
the new court of appeals an undue proportion of la
bor and of responsibility, and, with an intelligible
line of demarcation, would still leave the Supreme
Court in the possession of appellate jurisdiction over
all cases based upon contract, over all cases involving
the titles or possession of real property, and. except
as to the single instance of divorce, over all cases
sounding in equity. Each court would possess aux
iliary and incidental powers essential to the full exer
cise of its jurisdiction, and, on the point of jurisdic
tion alone, the court of appeals would be under the
control of the Supreme Court, and thus conflict and
confusion would be prevented. In this connection,
moreover, uniformity of decisions would be secured
by a provision' that the rulings of the Supreme Court I
heretofore and hereafter on all questions of practice
and procedure, wherever applicable, shall be binding j
authority in the new tribunal.
The effect of these suggested amendments to. the
constitution would be a great reduction of the volume
of business in the Supreme Court, which would still
retain the most important litigation, affecting com
mercial and financial interests, and, if the present
commission should be continued for four years, it
is confidently anticipated, would be abreast of its
calendar. The court of appeals,. unincumbered when
it started and with its labors held within reasonable)
limits, would be enabled to insure prompt decisions
of the appeals committed to its charge.
It has been suggested that the constitution itself
should provide for the oral argument of all appeals,
and that when a limited number of cases arc sub
mitted further business should be deferred until they
are decided. There are several valid objections to
this detailed specification of methods, however meri
torious in itself. It is clearly â an introduction of
special legislation into an instrument that ought to
consist only of broad outlines.,' It is also an unwar
ranted interference with a discretion that should be
vested in the Judges, and not even in the Legislature.
They are trusted with specific duties, and it is for
them to determine for themselves and through the
rules their knowledge and experience may enable
them to formulate in what manner their work can be
most expeditiously and effectively acomplished. The
community will look to results and will not closely
scrutinize mere preliminaries.
It is certainly strange that the Filipinos should
have 600 American soldiers as prisoners without our
commanding officers missing them at rollcall.
The peculiar form of wit which finds expression in
mutilating the property of a university is something
which should be attended to by the police.
The country will be glad to learn that the packers
of the suspected meat are so confident of its goodness
as to freely swear to it. - -
The police have lately been reasonably successful
in capturing burglars, but there are still too many
mashers at large. 'â¢". -. r -~ '- _\'-~:
The Examiner still maintains its reputation as a
blunderer and an egotist 'â ' -3/~ -'-._ â 'â â¢â¢:
SHE SIGNED
A DEED WITH
HER LEFT HAND
Carrie E. Sankey Tells
Her Story.
AFFLICTED WITH PARALYSIS
TAKEN ADVANTAGE OF WHILE
IN THIS CONDITION.
Charles S. Coggins Now Admits That
BHe Never Gave His Ward
Money From .the Profits j
of Her Estate. I
After a brief hearing yesterday after
noon Judge Belcher continued the case of
Carrie E. Sankey against her guardian,
Charles 8. Coggins, and his wife, Mary E.
Coggins, until the 14th Inst. Though
brief the hearing elicited facts of inter
est regarding the peculiar transactions
that took . from the young plaintiff her
rights and her property.
Miss Sankey told, the story on Wednes
day of how the profits from her estate
went to benefit her guardian's wife and
furnish luxuries. Yesterday she was
again placed on the stand, and out of the'
tangle of questions and answers the fact
was drawn that one of the deeds signed
by the witness conveying property to her
guardian was prepared and afterward
signed by Miss Sankey when she was suf
fering from paralysis and weak in mind
and body. She was deceived and led to
sign this document, she says, and when
she finally yielded' and was about to af
fix her name the pen fell from her paral
yzed right hand and she was forced to
use her left. The signature on the dead
bears out her statement, as. it- has a back
ward slant and theâ lines are in a trem
bling, broken hand.
After reciting the story of the signing
of the deed when she was partially par
alyzed the young woman gave an ex
planation of the matter in which her
guardian led her to part with her estate.
In addition to the threatened expose, as
told on the stand the first day, Miss San
key said that her guardian and his wife
told her that she had heart disease and
that she might fall dead any day, and in
sisted on always accompanying her
whenever she left the house. They would
not allow her to receive callers unless
Mrs. Coggins was present and took care
that, she never communicated with any
one regarding her estate or her life with
her guardian.
"We went shopping together," said the
witness, "and although Mrs. Coggins al
ways ordered everything I always paid
the bills." Many bills were introduced
In evidence showing purchases of house
hold effects, bric-a-brac and other things
which had been paid by the witness. One
was for the amount of $1047 for goods
purchased, and outside of two Items,
amounting to about $30, Miss Sankey said
that she knew nothing.
In conclusion the.- witness, in order to
show the attitude of her guardian and
his wife toward her, said that one night
she went to see a- lady friend and spent
the night with her. The next morning
Mrs. Coggins > called at the house where
the lady friend resided and said that if
Miss Sankey. did not open the door she
(Mrs. Coggins) would get an ax and
knock it down. Miss Sankey opened the
door and was roundly abused by Mrs.
Coggins. . ; '->.'_.â â¢-â 
â¢When Miss Sankey left the stand a dep
osition signed by Charles S. Coggins was
offered and. placed on record. This depo
t-lon bring- out one important factâ Cog
gins acknowledges that he never paid
Miss Sankey any money out of her es
tate. Miss Sankey alleges that he has
drawn out several thousand dollars and
now the. proof as to what for and where it
was expended is awaited with interest.

clusion with regard to the naming of the
lighter youngster. Each had mentioned
nearly every cognomen in the dictionary,
but none was thought suitable by the
other. V -i. V :
Their six-year-old boy, who had been
a patient listener during the friendly dis
pute and had been watching the subjects
of the conversation with feelings of
mingled wonder and mistrust, finally said,
when his father and mother gave up the
naming proposition as a bad job:
"Say. that's easy. If you're going to
call the big fellow John, why don't you
name the little one Demi- John?"

MAYOR PHELAN
WATCHES THE
NEW GAS BURN
A Comparative Test
of Illuminants.
NEW PRODUCT IS CHEAPER
CLAIMS MADE BY THE EQUIT
ABLE COMPANY.
It Will Try to Get the Contract for.
Lighting the Streets Along
Which Its Pipes
Are Laid.
An Interesting: test showing the com
parative illuminating powers of the gas
furnished by the Equitable and the San
Francisco gas companies was made yea
terday afternoon in the WÂ«JS*Â£S__
on Stockton street under the direction of
Dr. J. W. Chisholm. inventor of the gas
manufactured by the Equitable, and
Charles L. Ackerman, president of the
Th e a concern hopes to secure .the j
contract for lighting the streets alonÂ»
which its pipes are laid, and .accordingly
invited- Mayor Phelan, Gas Ins ec^ Tup
per and other city officials to judge for
themselves of the merits of the new prod
uct at a public test.
Before the experiments were made J_>r.
Chlsholm said a few words in explana
tion. "Our gas," he said, "is essentially
a fuel gas, and while we do not claim
that its illuminating powers are as great
as that of the old gas, we say that we can
furnish a good light far cheaper than the
old company. This test will be made by
what is known as the 'test meter. As
our gas will not burn with an open burner,
we will use a Welsbach lamp for both
Hl"thcn had the meter attached to one
of the tips, regulated it for a full light
and let it run for a minute. The result
showed that it was burning i.o feet
per hour. Another test made a few min
utes later with the cock turned on full
registered 4.6 feet per hour as a maxi
mum. This gas was drawn from a. chan
delier which had six other lights burning
on it. As the test of the San Francisco
Company's gas was to be made from a
single chandelier another record was
taken from a single chandelier of the
Equitable Company. It showed practically
the same pressure and the same consump
tion.
The product of the old company was
then submitted to a test with the same
meter and the same Welsbach. lamp.
There was no way of recording the candle
power, so the comparative brilliancy of
the two lights had to be guessed at. When
the register of the meter was taken there
was a difference of about 20 per cent, as
It showed that the old company's gas was
running through the meter at a rate vary
ing from 5.S to 5.5 feet per hour. _ *
President Ackerman was well pleased
with the showing made by his product;
"This test does not show as great 1 a dif
ference as some we have made," he said.
"We made a test- last Monday night which
was 25 per cent in our favor, but this last
one is very good. The record shows that
we furnish the same light with 20 per cent
less gas, and we only charge $1 per thou
sand, against $175 charged by the other
companies. Another feature is that our
gas is cleaner. I have here a number of
bottles containing sediment taken from
the pipes of the other company. Our gas
cuts this out and leaves the pipes per
fectly clean. Why, you can cook over our
gas with a silver pan and the bottom.of it
will not be discolored in the least.": .-â¢
ANSWERS TO CORRESPONDENTS.
HERMANSUL-W. A. TV., City. Her
mansul was a Saxon deity, worshiped in
Westphalia. Charlemange- destroyed the
idol and converted Its temple Into a Chris
tian church. The idol stood on column,
holding a standard In one hand and a bal
ance in the other. On its breast was the
figure of a bear and on its shield a Hon.
Probably it was a war god.
GEOMETRICAL FIGURE-E. N., City.
The geometrical figure asked about can
not be '"drawn with a pencil without lift
ing it from the paper or retracing a line"
In the ordinary way that would be implied
by the quoted words, but it can be done
by a trick; that ls, fold tho paper In such
a manner that when the pencil is drawn
across the paper to make one of the par
allel lines the side of the pencil will pro
duce another parallel line and the balance
of the drawing can be accomplished with
out removing the pencil from the paper
or retracing a line.
THE NAVEL ORANGEâ G., City.
The following is given in relation to the
origin of the seedless orange known as
the California navel: In 1873 the Depart
ment of Agriculture at Washington, D.
C, received several orange trees from
Bahla, Brazil, and they were sent to
Riverside, Cal.. where some were budded
on native trees. Nature attempted to
produce twins, but one of the twins sur
vived merely as a protuberance in the
blossom end of the fruit, forming a little
kernel enveloped In the skin. In the. pro
cess the seeds were never fully developed.
The horticultt lists tooit advantage of this
accident to extend the culture of the
seedless -fruit.
BLUING GUN BARRELSâ S. S., Dan
ville, Cal. The following ls given as the
methods for bluing gun barrels:
For bluing gun barrels by staining, dissolve
4*_ ounces of hyposulphite of soda in one quart
of water, also 1_ ounces of acetate of lead in
one quart of water. Mix the two solutions and
bring to a boil in a porcelain dish or stone pot.
Clean the gun barrel free from grease, oil or
varnish, warm the Barrel and smear with the
hot solution, using a piece of sponge tied to a
stick. When the color develops wash and wipe
dry; finish with boiled linseed oil.
The bluing of gun barrels is also effected by
heating evenly In a muffle until the desired
blue color is raised, the barrel being first
made clean and bright with emery cloth, 'leav
ing no marks of grease or dirt upon the metal
when the .bluing takes place, then allow to cool
In the air. It requires a great deal of experi
ence to obtain an even clear blue.

4 bitter harvest of disgrace, degrada- !
tion,*"dernora'ization and death from ;
the racetracks.' Homes are being |

llngleside.I Ingleside. The gates of San Quentin have
closed 'upon many men who were made ,
convicts by horseracing and the portals j
are open for many more who have lost t
honor and home in the dirty pool created !
In our midst by the horse-gamblers. Men :
hay- fled from their .... hide the
disgrace that came to them on the race
course. Some hav*r found oblivion and 1
escape from the sneers of honest men in
suicide. The public cannot estimate the
terrible damage that --â¢racing has in
flicted upon San Francisco and its citi
zens." : Sj-j v . \v-
These words were spoken yesterday by
one of the most prominent police official**
of this city. The remarks were prompted
â¢by the revelations and scandals which
have found their source in the, last month

r.sme to the black roll that racing gam
blers are writing for the people of San
Francisco.
When a Bernard Ward is place- behind
th_ -bars and some one whispers "the
bookmakers." the police point to the rec
ord of San Quentin and the Morgue. The I
officers are thinking of the McGlades, the
-Norton*, the Welbufns and the Wldbers,
types of hundreds whose lives have been
.wrecked or lost In -lie passion for gam- j
bling which the race tracks have bred in i
this city. r v
I The. evil has become of such terrible
moment that business men have been j
forced to employ spotters and send them
to the poolrooms and the tracks to watch
their employes. In public and private af- .
fairs the racing habit has played sad i
havoc. Merchants are afraid to trust
their own most trusted employes. In
public, offices, such as that of the County
Cterk. signs are conspicuously posted to j
warn deputies that dismissal will be the I
Immediate penalty for attendance at the
race tracks. A dread has seized upon the
custodians of money and the facts war
rant their fear.
The police estimate that in the last six ,
years over 300 men and a few women }
aye come under the observation of the â 
olice for crlme3 committed under the |

tat kept the bookmakers in business.

Not long ago two boys. Henry Walker
and Francis Dunn, broke into a room in a
Market street lodging house and stole a
coat, which they sold for $3. That theft
represented their interest in the gambling
at the local race course, as thousands
upon thousands represented the Interest
of Widber or Figel. The boys were caught
and arrested.' They admitted their guilt
I and declared they had lost all their money
on the races. The reward of their theft,

; to give them a new start in the evil game
â  that has led big and little men In this city

SOUSA AND HIS BAND.
Opening Concert Will Be Given at the
Alhambra Theater To-Night.
Sousa's famous concert band will open
at the Alhambra Theater to-night, and
music-lovers are promised another rare
treat. His concerts have always been dis
tinguished for their cheerfulness and
spirit. From the moment Sousa takes up
his baton the programme moves along
with a dash and whirl that is Infectious
and puts every one in sympathy with the
occasion. There are no depressing waits
or lapses and no time Is lost in giving
parting instructions; every moment is em
ployed in playing something for the pleas- j
ure of the audience.
Evening and afternoon performances ,
will be given both to-morrow and Sun
day. The soloists with the band are
Maud Reese Davis, soprano, and Dorothy i
Hoyle, vlolinlste.

ENGAGEMENT IS ANNOUNCED.
James Newlands Jr. and Mrs. A. S.
Hollister to Be Married.
Mrs. A. S. Hollister of this city publicly
announced her engagement yesterday to
James New-lands Jr.. nephew of Con
gressman Francis Newlands of Nevada.
Although the engagement of the couple
has existed for several months past, they
kept it a secret until the approach of the
weeding, which has been set for next
Wednesday at the home of the bride.
Mrs. Hollister is the daughter ot James
B. Stevens, assistant United States Ap
praiser for this port. She la well known
in social circles " and her engagement
comes in the nature of a surprise to her
many friends.
Mr. Newlands is the son of James New
lands, president of a number of the south
end Con-stock mines.. The groom Ijns
managed the land transactions of the
Sharon estate- at Burllngame and other
places for some time past.

through him began to bet on the races.
She made her tout her betting commis
sioner, and the firm for which she worked
was robbed of several thousand dollars,
which went into the coffers of the book
maker..

John L. Baumgardener. '.- Mason street,
swore to a complaint in Judge Graham's

son street whom he thought he recog
nized as an old acquaintance. The man
did not disabuse him of the Idea and they
had a number of drinks at Baumgarden
er's expense. They finally went to Baum- i
gardener's room, where they continued to !

She set fire to her home on Taylor street.
She was caught and convicted. When
asked what she Intended to do when she
Is released from prison, she replied calm

Mrs. Eugenia Schuller, who was ac
cused of arson, but was exonerated In
court of all guilt, declared to Fire Mar
shal Towe that she found . the keenest
enjoyment in playing the races. These
women are types of the women who may
be seen any day in the grand stand at
Ingleside.
Perhaps one of __< saddest of all the
tragedies that have their inception at the
race course and their conclusion at the
Morgue is that of William J. Lyons, who
was employed as a trusted bookkeeper by
the Pacific Gas Company. Lyons was led
gradually into the game that thrives at
Ingleside. He won and then lost. He
robbed his employers to recover his losses.
He lost again and stole more. His thefts
became so great that he was forced to
falsify his books. Having falsified his
records he was forced to destroy them,
and In desperation he set fire to the safe.
He was between $4000 and $5000 short in
his cash. Detection was certain and ha
fled from the city. The next day he was
found at Berkeley dead, with a bullet in
his brain. The searchers found twenty
six pool tickets in one of his pockets.
Lyons had played the races to a finish.
The story of Oscar Anderson is a short
one. He robbed Charles Nelson of â¢*_**-. a
month ago and disappeared. The book
makers have the money.
Robert Lawson is perhaps not yet for
gotten as the young man who was clever
enough to Impose upon E. M. Greenway.
Lawson was an inveterate gambler at the
racetrack. and consequently needed
money. He sold Greenway some bottled
water under the pretense that it was a
liniment. That offense was unpunished,
but it was not long before Lawson needed
more money. The races are hard to beat
as they are manipulated on the local
track. Lawson forged the name of Miller
& Lux to a telegram and filched several
hundred dollars from local firms. He was
arrested, tried, convicted and sentenced
to serve a term of two years at San
Quentin. His place of residence was
changed to the County Jail, where he is
now Imprisoned. In his conviction the
local racetrack lost a patron and a vic
tim.
Daniel Lynch, a young fellow who held
creditably a position as bookkeeper for the
firm of Anderson & Co., ship chandlers. is
another convict at San Quentin who owes
the tragedy of his life to Ingleside. Ha
was led to gamble on the races, embezzled
$1200 from his employers and is now serv
ing a term of imprisonment of three
years.
Bayard Saville was one of the pets of
the bookmakers. He was not only a good
fellow, but a good thing, and for his good
fellowship to the crafty gamblers of In
gleside he is now serving at San Quentin
a term of fourteen years for forgery- Sa
ville played the races heavily and lost.
He practiced a forgery upon Mrs. Bell,
was caught and then convicted.
Henry Sloan, who was notorious as a
gambler at the race track, is also at San
Quentin for a term of ten years. He
found it necessary to commit forgery to
play the game. Sloan is well connected
In this city and New York, but he was
utterly ruined by the masters of Ingleside.
The race course never showed itself
more conclusively a feeder for the peni
tentiary than It did in the cases of Ste
phen Rosenbaum, Dr. Starke, W. F.
Rodgers and son. who were all sent
to San Quentin prison as the direct con
sequence of their operations on the race
course. These men had played the races
and lost. They gave up their legitimate
employment, bet and lost all their money
and then schemed to fight the sharks of
the course with their own weapons. The
quartet of gamblers flooded the track
with counterfeit money. Federal detec
tives were placed on the case and the
counterfeiters were soon behind the bars.
Young Rodgers died in prison. His father
and Rosenbaum are still serving their
term. Starke, who is a member of a re
spectable family, was released about a
month ago.
It is not an infrequent Incident that
thieves bite at the game of thieves and
are caught. W. A. Williams, who Is serv
ing a term at San Quentin, is an exam
ple. -Williams is a notorious criminal. Ha
operated in this city and lost his ill-got
ten gains at the race track. He was tha
rascal who forged the name of Leland
Stanford.
J, E. Bachmann is one of the most re
cent victims whose crime has been discov
ered. He was treasurer of a local frater
nal society, and in. that capacity had
charge of a great deal of money. He
thought that he could play against the
bookmakers and he tried. He lost, stole
several thousand dollars from the society
of which he was treasurer and placed the
stolen atony with the bookmakers. De
was caught, tried, convicted and sen
tenced to serve a term of rive years at
San Quentin. He has learned bitterly the
lesson of the track.
John W. Jordan, formerly a porter in
the postoffioe. robbed the mails to play
the races. The game was with him as It
has been for so many. He lost and was
sent to the penitentiary for a year. He
was recently released.
Charles T. Jensen tried another road
that leads to the pool box. He passed sev
eral fictitious checks on local grocers.
His fraud was discovered. He was con
victed and is now at San Quentin for a,
term of two years.
M. Buja is another victim.
James D. Page is still another.
The list is a long one, replete with the
'. tragedy of life in many phases. The peo
! pie of San Francisco are paying dearly
for the luxury of the racecourse. .The
names that have been given do not by
any means complete the roll. They serve
- simply as types for the 300 that have come
beneath the scrutiny of the police and the
500 that have their secret of shame hid
den in the safes of private detectives.
HAS FOUI-ID THE AUTHOR.
James E. Slinkey Discovers the Wo*
man Who Wrote the Anony
mous Letter. ",,"â -* ...
James E. Slinkey yesterday notified Cor-
I oner. Hill that he had discovered the
author of the anonymous letter written
several weeks ago accusing Slinkey of
having murdered his wife.
The wife of Mr. Slinkey committed sui
cide at the Sausalito House on Ellis street
last January by hanging herself. After
the body had been burled Dr. Hill re
ceived a letter Intimating that the woman
had been murdered. Acting on this, the
i body was exhumed by the Coroner, and
after a careful investigation it was, found
that the allegation made in the letter was
false. Dr. Hill told Mr. Slinkey that if he
â¢ could find the author of the letter an ar
j rest would follow.
Since that time Slinkey has been work
ing quietly on* the case, and yesterday he
totk Dr. Hill some letters, the hand
i writing of which corresponds with that of
the anonymous letter. Mr. Slinkey gave
I the name of the author, who is a woman,
| to Dr. Hill, and the latter wrote the
i woman requesting her to call at the Cor
j oner's office this morning. In case the
' woman does not comply with the doctor s
request a warrant will be sworn out for
her arrest. Dr. Hill and Mr. Slinkey both
I refused to give the name of the- woman.

OUR ACCOUNT WITH SPAIN.
AND OUR STANDING AS TO EXPORTS AND
IMPORTS. j
The New York circular of Henry Clews says:
"It is true that some misgivings may seem
to be suggested by the fact that we shall soon
have to remit $20,000,000 to Spain on account of ;
the peace settlement. And it is quite possible ,
that this operation, coming outside the ordi
nary transactions of the exchanges, may in \
some measure harden the local rate of Interest. 1
And yet, for these extraordinary times, this la 1
a relatively small matter. The available re
sources for making the settlement are super
abundantly ample. Outside the gold reserve,
the treasury has an available cash balance in |
its vaults of 886,000,000 and 886,000,000 on deposit
with the national banks. There ls thus ample
______ for the treasury avoiding any demand ,
upon its depositories in this city calculated to
sensibly disturb the loan market; and what it
can do In that matter it is quite likely to do. j
There is in Europe, however, a still large out
standing floating indebtedness due on demand ,
to the United States. A slight advance in the ;
rate of interest here would make it a paying
operation to effect the 820.000.0-10 remittance
through bills drawn against these funds; la j
which case there would be no transfers of :
money In the operation, Spain collecting her |
award from our European debtors and the
operation at this end being confined to trans- ;
fers of deposits, with little or possibly no effect
upon the loaning ability of the New lork
banks This remittance to Spain therefore, â 
come when it may, Is not likely to cause any
noteworthy disturbance of the local money |
market.
"It begins to appear probable that we are j
entering upon an appreciable abatement of the
extraordinary surplus of exports over imports
that has prevailed for the last eighteen months.
The imports are gaining upon the figures of
several mdnths past. The January figures are !
about $10,000,000 below those of 1*95 and 1896
respectively, but they exceed those of 189S by
87.600,000 and those of 1897 by 87,100.000. The
exports for January exceed those for the like !
month in any previous year, being $7,500,000
above those of 1898, the next highest ante- j
cedent yearâbut they fall 818,200.000 below the
average for December, November and October,
which is no more than is due. however, to the j
usual curtailment of shipments after the close (
of the year. The January excess of merchan
dise exports over imports, however, has fallen j
to the standard of one year ago, namely $57,
--000 000 which is $25,700,000 below the balance -
fcr' December. $20,600,00-) below November, j
ja poo ivio less than October and 814.400,000 over 'â 
September. Still, the January balance is at I
the rate of 8685.000,000 per annum. These facts '
Indicate that the accumulation of balances to
our credit in Europe must have very much more â 
than sufficed for offsetting the extraordinary
return of home securities since the beginning
of January, and it seems reasonable to infer
that the liquidation of the settlement with
Spain must leave still a very large sum to our
ered't abroad, liable to be liquidated in gold j
upon our demand."
THE LUMBER TRADE.
Pacific Coast Wood and Iron thus reviews the
lumber- trade in February:
"The receipts of lumber, both redwood and
spruce, show quite an Increase, but there is
no change to note in prices. The foreign de
mand keeps up and the Humboldt mills are
prepared to keep up with their orders. The !
air is full of rumors of consolidations of various ;
redwood interests, but nothing really definite I
has come to the surface and may not for some
future time, but it cannot be denied that the j
redwood prospects are brighter than they have !
b*en for years, and it Is hoped that the manu- j
facturers will scon be on Easy street.
"As to shingles, the market is practically
in the same condition as when last reported ,
in Wood and Iron, but the time is imminent
when the strong, carefully worked Eastern field
will receive its attention, and then the red
wood shingle will be where its superior quali- I
ties should place itâ the head and above all ,
other classes and grades. The coming spring
must result in an influx of orders for a grow
ing Eastern demand has been naturally created.
"Hardwoods are where they were last month,
save that there seems to be an increasing local
demand and prices are firm and responsive.
The extreme climatic conditions prevailing j
during the month of February' have made the !
lumber business practically non est. Such is
the radical difference between the West and
the East, for the business in the lumber trade i
circles of the first named section is good an
encouraging and in the latter actually at a
standstill."

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
The weather is cloudy throughout Washing
ton. Oregon, Idaho, Nevada and Northern Cali
fornia; elsewhere on the Pacific Slope it is
generally clear. Light rain or snow has fallen
over the greater portion of the country west
of the Rocky .tains north of Central Cali
fornia ar.d Nevada. It is snowing In North
ern Nevada this evening.
Tie temperature has remained Stationary ex
cept in Northern Nevada and Southern Idaho,
where it has fallen.
Conditions are favorable for generally fair
weather in California Friday, with fresh north
west wind.

hours ending midnight March 3:

EASTERN MARKETS.
NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
J
NEW YORK. March The stock market
displayed weakness, particularly the Grangers
and specialties, practically all day with but
incidental rallies. There, was an appearance
of support of certain stocks In the final deal
ings which caused a fairly steady close, al
though the Grangers were still under pres
sure. There was no news to account for
the decline except a firmer tone to money,
call rates touching 3*_, attributable to call
issued preliminary to the settlement between
the Central Pacific Railroad and the Govern
ment. The easier tone of sterling exchange
was a natural accompaniment of the firmer
tone in money, indicating that an Increased
demand for money might contract, the volume
of American funds now loaned abroad. There
were denials from bank officials of the cur
rent assertion that undue discrimination was
being exercised against the Industrial collater
al. Selling orders appeared from Boston. and
Chicago houses, and London, while trading
both ways moderately, took rather less stock
â¢ than it sold. By the bear attacks, many
stop-loss orders were uncovered which ac
celerated the decline. The weakness ; affected
nearly everything, while especially heavy fea
tures were Metropolitan, Jersey Central,. Pacific
Mail, Pittsburg, C. C. and St. Louis, Rubber
and Brooklyn Transit. American Tobacco left
oft" with a gain of 2_ on the formal action
of the directors preliminary to the absorption
of the Union Company. ,-.?
The bond market to-day bore up remarkably
well, considering the pressure against stocks.
Some Issues made an aggressive show of
â¢U-Urth, particularly Louisville, Evansvllle
and St. Louis 6s, which rose 3 points. Atchi
son adjustments had good backing^ and the
St. Louis Southwestern issues were better sup
ported, than for some days. Operations else
where were somewhat irregular, but there were
no conspicuous losses outside of Southern Rail
way 6s, and Standard Rope and income.
The new Central Pacific and Southern Pa
cific bonds issued in connection with the re
habilitation of the Central Pacific Railroad,
were dealt in to-day. but ran off from the
high prices in sympathy with the weakness
of the connecting stocks. Total sales, 34,505,000.
United States 3_ and the os advanced % in the
bid price.

THE LONDON MARKET.
NEW YORK, March 2.â The Commercial Ad
vertiser's London financial cablegram says:
The markets here showed a universal lull in
speculation. Americans zigzag-ed within nar
row limits. London interest in them seems to
have vanished. They were bought slightly in
the morning, but the close was nearly at the
bottom. Paris bought some. Tintos were 39%;
Anacondas. 9 11-16. Money was in good de
mand.
