CHICAGO GRAIN MARKET.
CHICAGO. March 2.â AH the early news was
against the price and wheat started weak.
The weather was moist and mild, the Liver
pool market was %d lower, and the Price
Current's weekly summary said the wheat
plant was not Injured, to any widespread ex
] tent. Under such conditions May wheat com
j menced %_>% c lower at 73{g73"mc, and buyers
were not numerous at over 73c. Numerous
buying orders were received from country
points in Illinois and Indiana for wheat. The
market held stubbornly firm around opening
prices until about an hour from the start, but
after that selling Increased and finally, after

a severe struggle around 73c for May the bears
got the upper hand and broke the price down
to 72*Â£ c. Additional rains were falling: in Cali
fornia and it was generally believed that the
drought situation had been thoroughly relieved.
The market continued heavy with offerings in
excess of the demand until about twenty min
utes from the* close, when on â attempt of early
sellers to restore holdings the price shot up
rapidly. May closed at 72% c, a net decline of
%c.

CALIFORNIA RAISINS IN NEW YORK.
NEW YORK. March 2.â The Journal of Com
merce says: The impression among those here
who have closely watched recent developments
in the Coast raisin industry is that yesterday's
meeting would result in no definite action.
Postponement for a week or more would prob
ably hÂ» had, and during the interval every
effort would be made to secisre the co-operation
of recalcitrant growers. Even in the event of
final failure to reorganize the association, little
fear seems to be felt that the market will be
come demoralized by dumping upon it the bal
ance of the stock controlled by the association.
Officers of the association, it is claimed, have
virtually made a contract with every Jobber
who has bought not to permit the market to
decline below prices fixed by the association
during the balance of th* season, and it is con
fidently believed that they will carry out the
agreement made several weeks ago. when reso
lutions were passed to sell what stock re
maleiKi. If not taken by regular distributors.
to wineries or for export. The stock held by
1 the association is said not to exceed 600 cars,
though in pome quarters it is believed to be 100
or 200 carp more. Whether 600 or 800 cars, this
stock is understood to represent almost the
entire holdings of first hands on the Coast,
outsiders having little or nothing left. Last
year at this time the visible supply in first
hands exceeded 1000 cars. Thus, while there
may be no renewal of the growers' agreement,
'conservative pporl? believe there is little rea
son to expect there will be much if any decline
in prices should the combination not be con
tinued particularly, as present supplies in the
hands of distributers In the East are com
paratively light and the stock now in sight
will have to supply the market for at least six
months to come.

LONDON, March â There was a fair attend
ance at the sheepskin tales to-day. The sales

OREGON.
PORTLAND, Or., March 2.â The Eastern and
foreign wheat markets were lower to-day, and

market. The market though has been forced

I the pressing demands have been met and buy

wheat might have brought a cent more. Valley
and blue stem are nominally 60@61c.

THE STOCK MARKET.
Business was rather better on the Bond Ex
change and on the morning session Contra
Costa Water and Market-street Railway both
advanced to $65.
Mining stocks were off again and the best
prices were with few exceptions lower than
those of the preceding day. The report from
the pump was as follows: ,- The water has
been lowered to 50 feet below the 1760 level,
>r 77 feet below the Sutro tunnel discharge.
Several "stops were made ' during the past
twenty-four hours on account of shaft work.
The water is still going down." v ..
Sales of bonds on the Bond Exchange in Feb
ruary were $710,360, against $452,000 in Febru
iry, 1898. The sales of stocks were 63,472 shares,
against 54,475 last year. For the first two
months of the year the sales were $1,424,400 In
bonds, against $799,500, and 124,994 shares,
-gainst 91,105.
The semi-annual Interest on the Market
street Railway s's, Ferries and -Tiff House 6's,
Powell-street Railway 6's, Spring Valley Water
"s and Stockton Gas and Electric 6's is now
payable.
The Pacific Lighting Company will pay a
monthly dividend of 35c per share on the 6th
Inst.
The Alaska Packers' Association has de
clared the usual monthly dividend (No. 63) of
"5c per' share, payable on the 13th Inst. -"-'.
The official letter from the Justice mine
states that on the 370 level during the past
creek the work was confined to stoplng ore
from the south side in upraise No. 1. No. 2
upraise is up 32 feet. The face is in quartz
arrylng bunches of ore assaying $6 20 In gold
and 4.26 ounces of silver; total, $10 46 per ton.
During the week they hoisted 72 tons and 1800
pounds of ore, the car samples -if which aver
aged $19 23 in gold and 4 ounces of silver:
total, $21 60 per ton. The repairs to the south
drift on the 490 level wpre continued. At a
point 10 feet back from the face of the drift
in upraise has bef.-n started.

NOTICE TO MARINERS.
jr
A branch of the United States Hydrograpt.'c*
Office, located at the Merchants' Exchange. I. ' t
maintained in San Francisco for the benefit of
mariners, without regard to nationality and
free of expense.
Navigators are cordially invited to visit tha
office, where complete sets of charts and sail"
Ing directions of the world are kept on ha- i
for comparison and reference, and the latf.-t
information can always bo obtained regardlrg
lights, dangers to navigation and all matf>r 8
of Interest to ocean commerce.
The time ball on the tower of the new Fer .â 
building, at the foot of Market street. Is hoists^
; about ten minutes before noon and dropped at
noon. 12Cth meridian, by telegraphic signal re
ceived each day from the United States Naval
Observatory. Mare Island. Cal.
A notice stating whether the ball was dropp.-.^
on time or giving the error, If any. Is published
in the morning papers the following day.
W. S. HUGHES,

WAS NOT INSIDE THE MONEY
THE SCBAMBEE CAPTTJIIED BY
FLAM.EA.
The Hot Spring* Poolrooms "Killed**
Over Her Victory â Race
Will Bear Investigation*
Four Beaten Choices.
Fred Foster, the astute Eastern horse
man, paraded his 510,000 beauty, Yellow
Tail, at Oakland track yesterday. The
giant chestnut colt was asked to beat a
very moderate field, and opened a 1 to 2
favorite. Later 4to 5 and a trifle better
was obtainable. Piggott had the mount,
and the youngster displayed about as
much speed as a "yaller daw**," finish
ing a forlorn looking fifth, after sprawl
ing all over the track the last part. Fla
mora, the entry of Ed Purser, and 5 and
6to 1, won easily from Winyah. The
.latter, with Nash Turner In the saddle,
was the supposed good thing in the race.
As an excuse tor the favorite's miserable
showing. It was said that the going,
which was muddy and holding, did not
suit him. When owned by Burns &
Waterhouse he showed a decided ten
dency for damp footing.
Now comes a report from Hot Springs,
Ark., received last evening, stating that
the pool rooms there were "killed" over
the victory of Flamora, thousands of dol
lars being won by a certain clique on the
Purser entry. The bettors, so the ad
vices say, who executed the coup, were
the ones that*" made the big clean-up over
the win of Tammany Hall, when the Mon
tana horse downed Foster's sprinter
Abuse some days ago. On that occasion
Poster claimed to have lost a small for
tune on his horse, and because of his
defeat Cash Sloan, who piloted him, was
Indefinitely suspended.
. In the face of this latest information
It looks as if Sloan should have a rehear
ing, and two or three races of Mr. Fos
ter's horses ventilated with possible
pleasant results by the board of stewards
at the track across the bay.
The only other striking feature of the
sport was the run made by Peter Weber.
As good as 400 to 1 was laid against the
outsider, and he finished second to Good
Hope. Two or three books chalked up 10-3
to 1 that he would not run second. Two
favorites only were successful.
There was no pronounced favorite for
the 'opening seven furlong run, Carrie U
and Stan Powers dividing the honors at
post time. The latter ran third. At the
wire the 6 to 1 chance Rosemaid won eas
ily from Bliss Rucker.
rieber's gelding, Sweet William, next
gave the crowd a tired feeling. Closing
a mild choice for the sprint thai fol
lowed, he ended up in third place, after
closing up a lot of ground lost at the
start. Leading from the jump, Rieardo,
â¢a 4 to 1 chance, led New Moon over the
line a length.
In the fourth number, a six furlong

FAMOUS LIGHTWEIGHTS
WILL MEET TO-NIGHT
DAL HAWKINS and Frank Erne of Buffalo, N. T., will enter the Tin-s
--at Woodward's Pavilion this evening at 8:30 o'clock sharp. The
lightweights, according to agreement, will box twenty rounds. Haw
kins is a favorite with the local betting men, but Erne will have
plenty of Eastern money to back him when the pools open this evening.
Joe Choynski will referee the game and a better selection could not be
made. The battle should be well worth going a long distance to see.

COMMISSIONER NORTH
DISMISSES NINE MEN
AGREES TO DISCHARGE OF JAPS
INDICTED FOR BRIBERY.
Doubtful Whether the Men Could Be
Convicted, as They Had an
Ingenious Defense.
J. Tnri and Y. Nishloka, Japanese Immi
grants, pleaded guilty In the United
States District Court yesterday of having
paid money to Deputy Immigration Com
missioner Geffeney for the purpose of in
fluencing his decision as to their right to
land.
Tari and Nishloka were Indicted with
nine others by the Federal Grand Jury for
the offense named.; On the Immigrants
being lined up by the Commissioner in
the cabin, Tar! and Nishloka walked up
to the table at which Geffeney was sit
ting and each slipped a $5 gold piece Into
his hand under the table. The nine fol
lowed their example, but placed the $5
coins on top of the table. The two lead
ers, however, Informed Geffeney that
there would be more money coming after
they were landed.
At the request of United States Attor
ney Foote, â¢ represented, by Assistant
United States Attorney Banning, a nolle
prosequi was yesterday entered In the
case of the nine remaining prisoners. The
defendants engaged Bert Schlestnger to
defend them, and the ingenious defense
was set up that they had been informed
at Kobe by the agent of the steamship
company that they would be required to
pay a fee of $5 each to the Immigration
Commissioner before he would allow them
to land. Under this plea and the fact that
the men paid the money to Geffeney in an
open manner, and that they were entitled
to land, It was argued that a conviction
could not be had.
Mr. Banning, in pursuance of a rule of
his office, consulted Immigration Com
missioner North as to the course to be
pursued, and Mr. North, through Mr.
Geffeney, informed Mr. Banning that the
suggested dismissal of the cases would
be satisfactory to him. Upon this assur
ance, Mr. Banning went into court and
procured the dismissal of the cases.
The two defendants who pleaded guilty
will be sentenced on Saturday.

Dr. Tillman Back at His Post.
Dr. Tillman has returned to his post of
duty at the City and County Hospital
from the Penthouse, where he went to at
tend the two smallpox patients, who were
confined there some time ago. The pa
tients recovered and the young doctor
who volunteered to attend them met with
a hearty reception from his medical
brethren on his return. r - , '

ALAMEDA COUNTY NEWS.
SAYS LILLIAN
BRANDES DIED
OF PNEUMONIA
Remarkable Opinion
of Dr. Muller.
DEFENSE'S HOPES RUN HIGH
BRANDES WILL TESTIFY IN HIS
OWN BEHALF.
Queer Ideas on the Flexibility of a ,
Skull and Absence of External
Symptoms in Hang
ing.
;
Oakland Office San Francisco Call, i
908 Broadway, March 2. i
William A. Brandes, the accused raur- \
derer of his daughter, will bo given an I
opportunity to relate bis theory of his ;
daughter death to the jury. Mrs. Etta
Brandes. who is also charged with mur- â 
der, will also be called as a witness. This j
was decided upon late this afternoon by j
Attorneys Sawyer and Bennett, who have ;
been conducting Brandos' defense.
While interest in the now celebrated j
case lagged somewhat to-day, there were j
some most remarkable statements made j
by the medical expert- called by the de- I
fense.
Dr. G. L. Eaton, who had never exam- I
ined the body of the dead girl, testified j
that his office is next to that" of Dr. E. H. i
Woolsey, the defense's chief medical ex- j
pert; that he had carefully read the I
transcript of the testimony adduced at the ;
preliminary examination and that he dis- j
cussed the case with Dr. Woolsey at con- I
siderable length. He gave it as his opin
ion that Lillian Brandos had died by |
hanging: that the bruises. marks and mot- j
tled appearnce of the dead girl's limbs j
.were post mortem, and were evidence of |
hanging. 1*
On cross-examination Dr. Eaton gave
some remarkable opinions. He was hand- I
ed the skull cap of the dead girl, and i
regarded It as the skull of a person be- 1
tween 20 and 22 years of age. Lillian
Brandes was 13 years of age at the time I
of her death. He stated that the bones of !
the skull of an Infant, even at the time I
of delivery, could not be bent by Instru- j
ments, but that the skull would more |
Jikelv be broken. On the subject of hang
ing, witness stated that in all such cases
there is found congestion of the lungs
and mÂ«ir* or less congestion of the brain :
substance. He said as an absolute fact i
that In hanging there are commonly no ]
external symptoms, neither in cases of
suicidal hanging nor Judicial execution, j
and that death by hanging was caused by
the pressure on the pneumogastric nerve,
and that such pressure was heavier in :
suicidal hanging than in cases of judicial !
execution.
Dr. H. E. Muller, the next expert wit- \
ness for the defense, made perhaps the j
most startling statement from a medical ,
standpoint that has been given at the i
trial. He said that the adhesions of the !
membranes, as found in the case of Lil- j
lian, were due to pleuro-pneumonla, which i
caused the adhesions of the pleura in the I
lower half of the right lung; that the
meningitis which produced those adhe
sions was an incidental complication to the |
pleuro - pneumonia; that persons who
have this condition of the membranes, fol- I
lowing these cases of pleuro-pneumo
nla, are liable to be mentally and mor- j
ally disturbed, and that such cases are !
frequently found in insane asylums.
Dr. Muller went still further by stat
ing that the meningitis as found in the
case of Lillian Brandes. as shown by the
prosecution, was not sufficient to kill the
child. ; l
The defense now bases its main hopes
upon the theory that the prosecution must :
rely upon shock as the cause of Lillian's i
death, and that this shock, between the j
time of the infliction of the blow on the i
head and the time of death, nearly four
days later, would have spent itself and
would not have been present as a disturb
ing force at the time of death.
Dr. Muller will again occupy the stand
to-morrow, and thereafter the defense has
but two more medical experts to offerâ i
Drs. Pratt and Crowleyâ and early next ,
week the accused murderer and his ac- ;
cused wife will be called. It Is expected
the case will reach the jury for deliber- !
ation by the end of next week.
The weary trial is weighing heavily on
Brandos. His bearded face is decidedly >
pale his eyes have lost their luster, and
he appears to be laboring constantly un
der great mental strain and agony. At j
times he seems greatly bored, and to-day i
he was unusually nervous.
PLOT IN THE FELIZ
DIVORCE THICKENS
ATTORNEY CONNERS JOINED AS
A DEFENDANT.
Mrs. Feliz Charges Her Husband
With. Unscrupulous Trans
actions.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, March 2.
The plot thickens in the divorce suit of
Refugio Bernal Feliz against Joee Feliz,
on the ground of desertion, and defend
ant's attorney, D. M. Conners, is to be
made a defendant. Notice to that effect
was filed to-day by plaintiff's attorneys,
William Bailey and Carl Wood, who will
on next Monday also move that their
client be permitted to filo an amended
supplemental complaint.
The latter pleading will set forth that
when Refugio Bernal Feliz married Jose
on December 26, 1885. she possessed a 200
--acre ranch near Pleasanton, and, being
desirous of also owning two town lots In
Livermore, and reposing confidence in her
newly acquired Jose, she gave him money
wherewith to purchase for her the cher
ished property. Instead, Jose had an un
divided one-half interest vested in his
name, and after plaintiff had expended
$1000 in improvements on the lots Jose
mortgaged one-half . interest to D. M.
Conners as security for a promissory note
for $225. bearing date February 10, 1899.
The amended supplemental complaint
will also allege that on February 13 last
Jose, without any consideration, pretend
ed to convey by deed to Antonio B.
Mezzo an undivided one-half interest in
the property. Mrs. Feliz therefore asks
that Mezzo, too, be made a defendant.
Mrs. Feliz alleges that. she Is not versed
in the vernacular, and therefore did not
understand what her deserting husband
was about. She asks that deeds and mort
gage be declared void and that the court
decree the property as being held in trust
for her. -'"â ',"; '":%â

OAKLAND. March 2.â A marriage li
cense was issued by County Clerk Jordan
this afternoon to Andreas Fred Schneider
and Emelia Schneider, aged 51 and 53
years respectively, both residents of San
Francisco The couple have been married
" before, and several years ago were legally
separated. . _

VOTERS IN
THE ANNEX
QUITE SAFE
Dow Wins the Suit
for the City.
WILL INFLUENCE POLITICS
AUDITOR SNOW'S STAND ON
WATER PLANTS.
Many Campaign Meetings Held and
Other. Are Planned
Quiet Work Being
_- Done.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call, !
908 Broadway, March 2.
The 1500 voters In the annexed district '
have no longer any doubt as to their mu
nicipal standing, for the Supreme Court
handed down an opinion to-day declaring
that the annexed territory is legally a
part of Oakland.
The main point of contention was a i
technical one, based on the apparent il
legality of incorporating outlying terri
tory which was already divided into in
corporated sanitary districts. The Su
preme Court reviewed the matter at some
length and disposes of all the objections.
It was supposed from the first that the
annexation project having obtained the
necessary two-thirds vote, would be sus
tained by the court on the general prin
ciple that courts are always reticent
about upsetting the expressed will of the
people. The case was brought by Thomas
Cuff, who appealed from the decision of
the Superior Court affirming the annexa
tion. City Attorney Dow conducted the
case for the city. *â â â  V-;.
The political complexion of this new
territory will be tested on the 13th of this
month for the first time in a municipal
election. The district is claimed by both
parties, and if public opinion in the an
nex is of the same variety and as change
able as that of Oakland It would be hard
to guess which side has a majority.
The sorrow that has como to the home
of Mr. Snow cannot help but have a
marked effect on the political campaign
now pending. John L. Davie has stated
that it was understood between him and
Mr. Snow that this campaign should not
be one of vilification, and now that Mr.
Snow i_ perforce removed from the plat
form it is apparent that decency should
prevail and that little more real cam
paigning can be done.
Mr. Snow, prior to the death of his wife,
had prepared a speech which he Intended
to use through the campaign. This
speech has been given out for publica
tion and shows very clearly how* the Re
publican standard bearer stands on pub
lic questions. Regarding the important
question of Oakland's water supply, Mr.
Snow says:

SAVED BY TIMELY NERVE.
! Narrow Escape From Being Burned
to Death.
ALAMEDA. March 2.â By the presence
of mind this evening of Christina Olsen, a
domestic employed at the home of Lewis
E. Spear, 1625 Dayton avenue; Miss Anita
Lopez, a nursegirl, was saved from a hor
rible death. The young lady was seated
In the kitchen near a hanging lamp, which
fell from its fastenings and breaking on
the floor enveloped her In blazing coal oil.
j The frightened girl ran from the kitchen
I to the dining room, her clothing in flames.
! screaming with pain as the Are reached
I her body Miss Olsen, who was n the
â¢ front part of the house, was attracted by
I her piercing screams and ran to her as-
I sistance. Taking in the situation at a
glance Miss Olsen grabbed a table coyer
i and hurriedly wrapped It about Miss Lo
pez, smothering the flames.
I The injured girl's hair was singed, her
face considerably scorched and her right
i limb quite seriously burned. A physician
i was summoned, and after dressing the in
! juries stated that while the burns were
painful they were not of a very serious
nature. The girl's skirts and undercloth
! ing were burned almost to ashes, and her
' escape from death was miraculous. *
In the meantime the burning oil had set
j Are to the woodwork of the kitchen, which
! blazed briskly, but was extinguished by
I Louis Jacob! Jr. and Charles Wheat on,
! neighbors, before the arrival of the Sher
: man street hose wagon, which responded
! to a stil alarm sent in about 8:30 o'clock.
YOUNG MRS. LEVISON
DENIED A DIVORCE
UNABLE TO PROVE . LEGALITY
OF HER MARRIAGE.
Her Alleged Husand Is Accused of
Successfully Deceiving Her
for Fourteen Years.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, March 2.
Young Mrs. Levison has been denied a
divorce by Judge Ellsworth, because, ow
ing to an alleged deception on the part of
her second husband, she is unable to
prove that she was legally free to con
tract a marriage with Levison twelve
years ago. â¢'â¢â¢' "-
When a girl, a little over 14 years old,
Mrs. Levison was Induced In Lewis and
Clarke County Oregon, to marry a man
named Mullin. She lived with him in the
forests for a few weeks, and at the end
of that time they were so mutually dis
tasteful to each other that they parted
willingly. Levison came on the scene, and
being more agreeable to the girl made love
to her with complete success. Mullin was
perfectly willing that his bride of a month
should obtain a divorce, and both he and
Levison assured her, so she claims, that
the necessary legal steps had been taken
toward that end. Believing herself at lib
erty matrimonially, Mrs. Mullin married
Levison by contract, and the relatives on
both sides regarded them as husband and
wife for a period of twelve years, during
which five children were born. Finally
Levison made himself very objectionable
and left his home. Mrs. Levison brought
suit for divorce on the ground of deser
tion, but was not able to prove to the sat
isfaction of the court that she was di
vorced from her first husband thirteen
years ago.
A few months ago Levison obtained
some notoriety in this city by declaring
he had swallowed a bottle of strychnine
because of his matrimonial sorrows, but
Mrs. Levison's friends declare that this
was simply a subterfuge on his part to
obtain sympathy. His wife is now living
in San Jose, working hard to support her
numerous family, and Levison is in the
employ of the lighthouse service on Goat
Island, but is not doing anything to
lighten his wife's burden.

ANNEXATION
TO OAKLAND
WAS PROPER
The Decision of the
Supreme Court.
SALOON-KEEPERS DEFEATED
WILL HAVE TO PAY A BIG MU
NICIPAL LICENSE.
The Newly Acquired Territory Sure
to Cut an Important Figure
in the Coming City
Election.
The Supreme Court has rendered a de
cision in the big Oakland annexation case,
holding that the annexation is legal.
While the matter is directly of great Im
portance to the people residing In the old
Lorin and Golden Gate districts, as well
as to the residents of Oakland, the deci
sion is far-reaching, as it will apply to
many municipalities all over the State
where enlargement of municipal territory
ls or may be contemplated. The facts of
the case as presented to the higher court
i are interesting. ' '.'.â¢â 
After the annexation of a large '.iec
of territory on the north of Oakland to
the city in the manner prescribed by l:Â«w,
action was begun to have the proceedings
declared illegal and void. ; The Superior
Court of Alameda County decided that
the annexation was legal.
From all accounts, those who were most
bitter In opposition to annexation were
about thirteen saloon-keepers, who were
anxious to avoid the payment of the mu
nicipal liquor license of $400 a year instead
of $100, the amount of the license of the
outside districts. Acting upon the deci
sion of the lower court, all of the political
parties in the annexed districts nominated
candidates for the offices of the Oakland
j municipality, that are to be filled by the
election that Is to take place on the 13th
of this month. In the meantime, the ap-
I peal had been taken to the higher court,
and, realizing the necessity of having a
j decision to avoid getting matters badly
I muddled, the Supreme Court consented
I to advance the case Instead of letting It
I take its regular course and come up in
I a year or so. The final opinion was writ
i ten by Commissioner Hayne, and Chief
! Justice Beatty added a concurring opin
ion.
Commissioner Hayne briefly states the
case by saying that it was to determine
the validity of proceedings under which
Oakland claims the territory lying north
of her charter line to the town of Berke
ley. The annexed territory includes the
whole of Adeline sanitary district and
part of Golden Gate sanitary district, and
the contention was made that sanitary
districts cannot be annexed to an Incor
porated town or city under the act of 1889,
under which the proceedings in question
were had. Relative to this the court in
part says:
This contention is based upon the proposition
that two public corporations cannot exist in the
same territory when the powers conferred upon
each conflict one with the other, and that the
powers conferred upon each do conflict; that
sanitary districts cannot be dissolved by an
nexation proceedings under the act of 1889. but
only In the special way provided by the sani
tary act under which they were organized, and
if they can be annexed to the city it must be
under the statute providing for the consolida
tion of municipal corporations.
It may be conceded at the outset that sani
tary districts are public corporations, though
not designated as corporations by the statute,
and that all their powers, duties and privi
leges arc_uch as are incident to municipal cor
porations formed under the municipal govern
ment act or existing under freeholders' charters,
though not possessing many of the Important
powers, duties and privileges of the latter, and
that the same powers could not after annexa
tion be exercised by each In the same territory.
But if the statute permits territory embraced In
or covered by a sanitary district to be annexed
to a city municipal corporation of a higher
class and capable of exercising the same func
tions as well as others essential to municipal
government such statute contemplates a ces
sion of the powers of the inferior corporation to
the greater, and a consequent dissolution of the
former as a result of the annexation.
If the territory* embraced in these sanitary
districts had chosen to incorporate under the
statute as a town or a city instead of becom
ing annexed to the city of Oakland it cer
tainly could not have been necessary as a pre
liminary to such Incorporation to disincorpo
rate as such sanitary districts, since by the in
corporation they would have preserved every
right and privilege they had before, and would
at the same time acquire other rights and
additional powers which would enable them
the better to preserve and secure all the rights
they had as sanitary districts; and jif > they
might incorporate as a town or city without
first dissolving the prior incorporation, no rea
son is perceived why they could not accom
plish the same thing by becoming annexed to
an existing municipal corporation, under the
act of 1889.
This statute unquestionably authorized the
annexation of the territory here in question un
less appellant's further contention can be sus
tained, viz.: that these districts were munici
pal corporations, and the proceedings to ac
complish the union of this territory with the
city of Oakland should have been taken under
section 8 of the act of 18S3.
The court holds that the suggestion
that difficulties might arise out of the
division of Golden Gate Sanitary District,
a part of which is not annexed, need not
cause apprehension, as the act providing
for the creation of Such districts also
provides for their dissolution.
The appellant's second point is: "There
is no law under which to add the an
nexed territory to any wards, so as to
enable the duly qualified electors residing
In the annexed territory to use the elec
tive franchise to which they are entitled
under the constitution."
To this the court answers by calling
attention to the Oakland charter, which
provides that the city shall be redls
trlcted Into seven wards every ten years,
so long as this is not done within ninety
days previous to an election. In altering
the boundaries of the wards, or creating
new wards, regard must be had to the
number of inhabitants, so that each ward
shall contain, *as near as may be, an equal
number of inhabitants, exclusive of per
sons incapable of citizenship in this
State. ______
The City Council adopted an ordinance
changing the boundaries of the Second
Ward, and the contention was that It had
no power to do so. â  .. â¢â _*â¢_;_*
On this -point the court, speaking of the
power given by the Legislature to make
such changes, says:
It is enacted that the boundary of the ad
jacent ward or ; wards shall be changed so as
to Include the added territory. As the city of
Oakland is limited to seven wards by Its char
ter the annexed territory was properly added
to "the Second Ward.- This. I think, could be
done without violation of Its charter. Section
25 of the charter, while requiring that the city
shall be redistrlcted Into seven .wards every
tenth year, does not prohibit a change of
boundaries at other times.
It seems that there were two petitions
for annexation, the first being Imperfect
in its designation of the boundaries of
the new territory- The second was acted
upon by the Council. The appellant ar
gued that in this there was irregularity.
The court held that no one was Injured
by the failure to submit the first to a I
vote. The lower court was sustained.

SWAIN WOULD
NOT KISS AND
BE FRIENDS
Architect's Wife Sues
for Divorce.
THE CHARGE IS DESERTION
NO CONTEST AND NO SENSA
TIONAL DEVELOPMENTS.
Parties to the Suit Amicably Settle
Out of Court All Questions in
Connection With Division
of the Property.
There are to be no sensational develop
ments in the Swain divorce suit. The
complaint which has just been filed by
Mrs. Wilhelmina Swain charges the hus
j band, Edward R. Swain, the architect,
â¢ with desertion, and the many charges
gossip has been wont to promise when
the suit came to trial are completely ig
nored.
The troubles of the couple have been
more or less freely discussed by their
friends for something more than a year.
At the beginning of the estrangement
there was some mention of a woman in
the. case, but the mysterious female has
since been lost to sight. In fact, those
in a position to know declare that Mrs.
Swain was most anxious for a reconcilia
tion with her recalcitrant spouse,, but
i that ail her overtures to kiss and make
up were ignored. Judge Denson of the
firm of Denson, Oatman & Denson, Mrs.
Swain's attorney, is authority for the
statement that the proceedings for di
vorce are just the natural result of a
I "cooling off" on both sides.
. The suit will not be contested. It is un
| derstood that the matter of property di
! vision has been satisfactorily settled by
i the unhappy couple and a large sum set
! tied upon the wife, so that the question of
I alimony ill not be raised by her.
The Swains were married March 28, 1888.
I The fruit of the union was one child,
who died several years ago. Mr. Swain
] is best known locally as the architect
j of the ferry depot. He is represented In
the suit by Linforth & Whittaker.
Mrs. Swain has a residence at 412 Leav
! enworth street, but spends- the greater
' Bart of her time with her sister, Mrs.
I >alv, at the latter's Oakland home. Mr.
: Swain is keeping bachelor's hall at 500
| Clayton street. .

A very important meeting of the As
sociated Students will be held to-morrow
morning for the purpose of considering
two or three radical changes in the or
ganization and scope of the student body
as a whole. The executive committee has
formulated three questions which they
will present for discussion. They read as
follows: "Shall there be an organiza
tion of the entire student body recognized
by the faculty and Board of Regents as
a part of the administration of the uni
versity?" "Shall the Athletic Association
be consolidated with the Associated Stu
dents, leaving athletic affairs to be man
aged oy 'i committee of the lattrt-'s ap
pointment?' "How shall the finances of
the organization be conducted?"
The far-reaching effect of these pro
posals will make their discussion of great
moment to the students. It is expected
that the meeting accordingly may lead to
consequences which will have an impor
tant bearing upon the future organization
of the body of Associated Students.

NO WARRANT YET
ISSUED FOR SHARPE
HAS NOT RETURNED TO HIS
LOCKED OFFICE.
Miss Jacobson's Ante-Mortem State
ment in District Attor
ney's Hands.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, March 2.
Dr. C. J. Sharpe is in no danger of ar
rest. He has not been at his office on
Washington street since he knew that
Miss Jacobson had made an ante-mortem
statement directly charging him with be
ing responsible for her death, and It has
been frequently reported that he is elud
ing the police. It was learned to-day that
the police* are not looking for him and
that the warrant supposed to have been
issued is not in existence. It Is known
that Dr. Sharpe was at his. office the day
after Miss Jacobson's death and it is
believed that he is now in San Fran-
Cisco.
The Coroner's jury did not bring In .
verdict charging Dr. Sharpe with the
murder of Miss Jacobson, and it was gen
erally understood that a warrant had
been issued from the District. Attorney's
office. No warrant has been issued by
either the District Attorney or the Coro
ner, and therefore no search, is being
made for the missing doctor. Should he
return to his office It Is likely that a
warrant would be Issued, but no one has
taken the Initiative.
The ante-mortem statement of Miss Ja
cobson in the possession of the District
Attorney was not presented at the Coro
ner's Inquest and its contents were not
known to the jurors.
DISPUTE OVER THE BALLOT.
All Alameda Candidates Likely to Be
Designated as Independents.
ALAMEDA, March 2.â is probable
that when the Alameda voter goes to
stamp his ballot at the April election he
will be unable to distinguish a Municipal
League candidate from a Harmonic Hall
nominee. All candidates will be alike to
him, for they will, in all probability, be
designated only as "Independents."
When City Clerk Lamborn announced
that he Intended to give all nominees and
parties a fair deal on the official ballot
Attorney R. B. Tappan threatened to apÂ«
ply to the Superior Court for a writ of
prohibition if he attempted to place the
designation "Municipal League" after the
names of any of the office seekers, claim
ing that the league- was not a recognized
political body under the law.
"If the question is submitted to the
courts," said the City Clerk to-day, "I
believe they, will decide in favor of Tap-
San's contention. But in retaliation the
lunlclpal League people will strive to
keep any mention of Harmonle Hall- off
the ballots on- the same grounds that
Tappan advances, and I think the courts
would sustain the league also."
The league appointed a committee at
Its last meeting to arrange to take the
proceedings indicated by Mr. Lamborn if
it was thought advisable.

CHINESE FISHERMEN
CAUGHT RED HANDED
Crew of a "Junk" Placed Under Arrest
by Constable Creed of Sausalito for
Using Prohibited Nets.
Celestials Bewail Their Fate and Protest That
They Have Been Paying $10 a Month Per
Boat for Protection.
A Chinese fishing junk was towed Into
Bausalito harbor last night with a strange
crew at helm and sheet. Instead of the
usual Celestial* navigator in charge. Con
stable Creed of Marin County did the
steering. The Chinese crew were prison
ers, and they were content to remain in
the' hold, cuddled up among the shrimps
and small fish which proved their undo
ing.
The crew, which consisted of six able-
CAPTURE OF THE CHINESE FISHING BOAT OFF McNEARS LANDING.
bodied yellow seamen, were caught in
open and active violation of the fishery
laws of this State by Constable Creed,
who will swear to a complaint against
each of them this morning before the Jus
tice of the Peace at Sausalito. The
Chinese were lodged in the Sausalito jail
last night.
Creed's charges against the Mongols are
that they used the ordinary bag netâ

Grace- Burt and May I-eelle, two young
â¢women whose fondness for the bright side

the "tanks" of the City Prison with a
charge of. petty larceny hanging over
their pretty heads. They owe their In
carceration to the ungallantry of "Pop
Sullivan, proprietor of the Hoffman cafe,
â¢who temporarily lost an overcoat through
their alleged theft.
A few nights ago Mr. Sullivan enter
tained the ladies named with. bumpers of
â¢wine In a back room of his establishment.
Sad to relate, his guests became Ine
briated, and when he was called to the
telephone by one of his employes they re
paid his hospitality by leaving with his
handsome topcoat. "When he returned and
found his friends gone and his overcoat
missing, he became angry, and hastily tel
ephoned to police headquarters that his
overcoat had been stolen by his visitors.
Detectives Dinan and. Reynolds were de
tailed on the case, and on Wednesday
evening met the culprits down town and
placed them under arrest. Despite their
tears and assertions that they took
"Pop's" coat in lest and had It In their
room on Ellis street, the hard-hearted of
ficers took them to the prison and placed
them on the mysterious small book.
The overcoat was found in the fair pris
oners" room and returned to the still
â wrathful owner. Mr. Sullivan informed
the detectives yesterday that he would
make complaint against the ladies, but up
to last night had not carried out his In
tention. -*
Friends of the prisoners, learning of
their unhappy plight, appealed to Attor
ney Archie Campbell, and ho has Inter
ested himself In the prisoners' case. If
the ladles are not booked to-day for their
alleged crime Mr. Campbell Intends ap
plying for a writ of habeas corpus from
the Superior Court to compel the police to
charge them, so that they can be re
leased on ball. The well-known attorney
ha a often threatened to test the right of
the police to confine people who are under
suspicion, as he claims it Is an unjust
practice and smacks of Siberian methods.

ity both for shrimp and small fish, the
latter of which they catch in abundant
quantities and do not return to the water
as the law demands. This ruthless depre
dation on the young fish in the San Fran
cisco and San Pablo bays during the past
few years has resulted in such depletion
of the various species that formerly
stocked these waters that the entire com
munity has become seriously alarmed.
The Legislature has passed laws prohib
iting such fishing, and the Fish Com
missioners have from time to time exhib
ited a desultory activity in suppressing it.
This practice has been constant, and the
reason of It is that the Chinese fishermen
have been practically unmolested by the
authorities, who instead of enforcing- the
law appeared to have winked at its open
violation. In fact it is stated by the

Deable and Smith Discharged.
William Deable and William Smith, of
South San Francisco, who were arrested
on a charge of robbing the Sierra Point
House at Baden, were discharged from
custody yesterday. The preliminary ex
amination satisfied the Justice of the
Peace that the young men had nothing to
do with the robbery.

deadly weapon. -. He was arrested by Po- i
liceman Beach for being drunk and drew j

Several" of the Chinese merchants en
gaged in the fishery business claim -they
have been making monthly payments of
! sums ranging, from $7 50 to $12 for every
boat for protection. This money was not
! collected by the deputies, but was paid
into the hands of certain attorneys of the
. Chinese for discreet distribution where it
| would do the most good. .
There are at least thirty boats in the
! bay employed for just such purposes as
I the one seized yesterday. On an average
] of $10 a boat this amounts to $3600 a year,
I which is divided among the attorneys.
: The problem to be solved Is who gets the

The' fishing smack taken by Constable
Creed yesterday was gathering fish from
nets stretched opposite McNear s land
ing a Chinese camp on the Marin shore
of San Pablo Bay eighteen miles from
Sausallto. Creed boarded the smack,
watched the Chinese untie their bag nets
and deposit in a common receptacle the
shrimp, commingled with young fish of
every description. To keep within the
pale of the law, the Chinese would have
had to throw back in the sea all the fish
caught. This they did not do. but con
tinued dragging in the nets and clearing
them of their burdens. After spending
an hour on the boat, and while the crew
were beating homeward. Creed placed
. them under arrest and towed the boat
and crew to Sausalito.
â¢ The head man among the fishermen
: gave the name of Ah Lee, and said he
was in the employ of Qwong Lee Chong,
806 Commercial street. He was surprised
at the arrests. His limited understanding,
, could not comprehend why he should be
I placed under arrest when he paid $10 a
1 month lust to avoid such an unpleasant
procedure. At first he objected to the
arrest, and ran his hands lovingly through
his pile of shrimps to show that he had
no fry with it. Then he thought better of
it and began to separate the fish from the
shrimps and cast them overboard. Violent
threats from t..e corporeal Constable
brought Ah Lee to a realization of the
proprieties, and he accepted the Inevita
ble with true Celestial Indifferenceâ
cept for a wailing complaint for his mis
placed monthly .10.
ANONYMOUS LETTER
SENT TO THE MORGUE
THE AUTHOR SAYS MRS. H. C.
RASMUSSEN WAS POISONED.
Claims Her Husband Murdered Her
in Order That He Might Marry
Another Woman.
A mysterious anonymous letter was re
i ceived by Coroner Hill yesterday morn
! ing advising him to make an Immediate I
j and thorough Investigation as to the I
| cause of the death of Mrs. H. C. Ras
mussen, who died, according to a duly
signed doctor's certificate, of blood I
poisoning, last Tuesday morning at her j
home, 1,844 Union street. 'â â¢ ;-.
The writer avers that the deceased did i
not die as a result of natural causes, but '
was poisoned by her husband, who took
this means of getting rid of her that he
j might marry a Mrs. Dolan, with whom,
it is claimed, he has been keeping com
pany for several months past. In proof
of this the letter states that the day
Mrs. Rasmussen died her husband went
buggy riding with the Dolan woman. This
in fact is true, but Mrs. Dolan did not,
according to her statement, accompany
Rasmussen on a ride of pleasure, but on
one of sorrow. She maintains that the
grief-stricken husband of the deceased
visited her at her home and made a piti
| able request that she accompany him to
an undertaker's ln order to select a
shroud and casket for his wife. She did
so, as she claims, reluctantly, not caring
to have the neighbors get a wrong Im
pression of her mission. After they left
the undertaker's she, according to her
own statement, drove to Cypress Lawn
Cemetery for the purpose of purchasing
a burial plat. The object of the letter, It
is surmised, is an attempt to satisfy the
author's animosity or spite against either
or both the Dolan woman and Rasmussen
by blackening their characters in the
sight of the public. -;â â ':__.Â«
From every Indication Rasmussen was
essentially a home man. From the state
ments of neighbors and friends he had
his affections there and was always actu
ated by the desire to make his home, as
far. as it was in his power to do so. a
happy one. .
The only theory that is advanced in
explanation of the letter Is the unusual
Interest which Mrs. Dolan exhibited of
late for the Rasmussen family ln general.
She had made presents to the children
I and had bought delicacies, etc., for the
i deceased woman during the period of her
Illness. 'â 'â¢*- '...-* â .*.-- *. :."'â¢:
Another passage of the letter states that
I the husband of Mrs. Dolan was forced to
â¢ | leave her some time ago on account of
: ! the attentions which were accepted by her
. ! from Rasmussen. This, however, is de-
I nied. Dolan was compelled to leave his
! wife so the latter said, because he re
j fused to support his children and insisted
1 in enjoying a life of absolute indolence.
1 I Since the separation it is conjectured that
! he shipped as a stowaway aboard of an
' Alaskan steamer. ...
1 Dr. W. Winterburg, who attended the
deceased from the time she was taken
1 sick until she died, scorns the idea that
: death resulted from anything other than
! natural causes. Pie has sumbltted. a
signed certificate of death, which^ls. now
! in the possession of the Coroner. '
Rasmussen emphatically - denied that
i there was any foundation for any of the
i charges made in the anonymous commu
nication. ; .â¢'â -'â¢â â â¢â â¢,'â _-_ .â¢' _â / -, V V\ 'â¢

GUILTY OF FRAUD
THE FOX SUIT AGAINST HIM
WITHOUT FOUNDATION.
Supreme Court Says the Capitalist
Was Not Responsible for the
Mining Official's Actions.
The case of Theodore Fox against Jonn
W. Mackay and the Consolidated Cali
fornia and Virginia Mining Company was
decided yesterday by the Supreme Court
in favor of the defendants. Fox â¢is a
stockholder In the mining company, and
Mackay was a director in the Nevada
Bank. In the complaint Mackay was ac
cused of conspiracy to defraud the min
ing company, with the aid of Its board of
directors, out of $50,000. The action was
to compel him to replace that sum of
money in the treasury of the company.
The basis of the cause of action was
found in the payment to W. H. Patton,
superintendent of the mine, of. the $50,000.
which was made in four monthly install
ments of $12,500 each. The money was
paid to Patton under a resolution or the
board of directors reciting that it was
done in consideration of the extraordi
nary and valuable services rendered to
the * company in extinguishing a fire
of long standing in the lower levels of
the mine. . . -'--Â* 7: '
In the lower court Mackay won the
case, from which two appeals were taken,
one from the judgment and the other
from the order denying a new trial. Jus
tice Garoutte. who wrote the opinion,
states: "Those findings of fact (of the
lower court) are to the effect that Mackay
committed no fraud; that he was not. a
conspirator; that he was not a member
of the board of directors of the mining
company; that he in no way profited b**
the transaction, and was an entire
stranger to the whole matter from start
to finish. Upon this state of facts a judg
ment of non-liability upon his nart has
ample support and should be affirmed. It
is so ordered." -; /

IN HONOR OF EMMET.
Governor Gage Will Preside at Satur
. day Night's Celebration.
The committee of arrangements for the
celebration of the anniversary of the birth
of Robert Emmet, Ireland's favorite mar
tyred son, is making elaborate prepara
tions for the entertainment to be given. at
Metropolitan Temple Saturday evening,
March 4. All necessary details were ar
ranged at- an enthusiastic meeting held
in the Knights .of the Red Branch Hall
last evening. A splendid programme will
be presented on this occasion. Governor
Gage will preside and Mayor Phelan will
deliver -an oration. - -v -v*-v '-'

TWO CAPTAINS AND
THE PILOT CENSURED
LICENSES OF A CAPTAIN AND
PILOT SUSPENDED.
Captain Gustafson of the William D
Severely Reprimanded for the
Recent Collision.
Captains O. F. Bolles and John K.
Bulger, United States local inspectors of
steam vessels, yesterday suspended the
license of Captain Clatrvllle of the ferry
steamer Oakland for three months, and
of Pilot J. H. Douglass of the sain, vessel
for twelve months for running down the
gasoline launch William D on the 22d of
January of this year. Two passengers
on the launch were drowned on that oc
casion.
The inspectors say in their report that
had Douglass signaled to stop and back
when he first saw the launch getting
nearer to the ferry boat be would have
avoided the collision. Captain Clalrvllle
was reprimanded for noâ¬ keeping a proper
lookout on that occasion. "Had he done
so," say the Inspectors, "he could not
have helped seeing the launch, as the
weather was clear?' ._._-- _.'â;.:
Captain Gustafson of the launch was
blamed also for the same reason, but the
fact that the . launch was less than fif
teen tons, and therefore not under the
jurisdiction of the inspectors, saved his
license. "Had Captain Gustafson," say
the inspectors, "been keeping a proper
lookout he would have seen the Oakland
In time to avoid the collision. We there
fore hold him to be as much to blame in
this matter as the pilot of the Oakland
and equally responsible for the collision."
PROFESSOR HAMMON RETIRES.
Forecast Official Willson Temporarily
in Charge of the Weather.
George H. Willson assumed charge of
the local weather bureau in the Mills
building yesterday, vice W. H. Hammon.
who has resigned to take a position in a
Pittsburg gas concern. Mr. Willson has
been in the office since 1890 and has been
temporarily in charge of Weather affairs
several times. He will make way for A.
G. .McAdie of New Orleans-, who has been
here before and has been reassigned to
this station by the Department of Agri
culture.
Mr.<W r illson says that there will probably
be rain ln Northern California to-day, and
it will extend to San Francisco. The con
ditions are such, that he predicts a storm
from the north, and believes that Califor
nia will yet receive a good downpour.
In many places In the State it drizzled
yesterday. The district about Fresno was
dry but cloudy. Between this city and
Santa Clara county very little rain has
fallen.
Another School Suit.
Rose M. Stack, who. for a few weeks
after her appointment as a teacher, such
appointment having been made December
14 1898, taught a class in Buena Vista Pri
mary School, but who was lately removed
by the new Board of Education, filed an
application in the Superior Court yester
day for a writ of mandate to compel the
board to reinstate her. Upon the filing of
the complaint an alternate writ was is
sued, returnable next week.
MRS. MARY "CULLEN"
HAS TWO HUSBANDS
THE FIRST WAS DIVING WHEN
SHE MARRIED.
Judge Belcher Therefore Decides
Against Her in Her Suit
for Maintenance.
Mrs. Mary "Cullen" Is not the legal wife
of Gerald Cullen. A few days ago an ac
tion instituted by Mrs. Cullen against the
man whom she thought was her husband
for maintenance was tried and submitted.
During the trial it was contended by Cul
len that when he took the marriage vows
with tho plaintiff she had a husband liv
ing In the person of one James Riggs. The
plaintiff denied the allegation and pre
sented a photograph of a ghastly Morgue
subject which she identified as Riggs. the
subject having been buried prior to her
marriage to Cullen. Many witnesses,
however, testified that they had seen
niggs as late as December last, and as
the plaintiff married Cullen, as she
thought four years ago, the marriage was
naturally illegal, for If Riggs was alive
last December he manifestly was four
v Judge Belcher, after giving judgment for
Judge Belcher, after giving judgment for
the defendant, said that the court could
not annul the marriage, as asked by the
defendant, stating that for that purpose
another action would have to be brought.
In all probability this will be done, and
doubtless Cullen will be freed from the
woman who claims to be his wife, but
whom the court has decided to be the
wife of another. Practically she Is not a
wife at all, for no husband will assume
the responsibility of calling her "wife."
LANDSBERGER-HERZ.
Brilliant Violinist and the Daughter
of Dr. Cornelius Herz to Wed.
Nathan Landsberger. the well known
violinist, and Miss Irma Herz, teacher of
music, will in the near future play a mat
rimonial duet of their own composing. In
other words, they will become man and
wife.
The bride-to-be is a daughter of Dr.
Cornelius Herz. at one time a millionaire,
who was mixed up in the Panama canal
scandals in France. He lost his fortune
and died in Bournemouth, England, only
eight 'months ago. The widow and three
daughters reside in London. Raoul, the
only son, is an undergraduate of Cam
bridge University. Irma and her young
est sister, Sibyl, c_me to this city four
months ago 'and took up their residence
with their uncle, Louis Saroni, the candy
manufacturer.
Miss Irma Herz has studied music in
London, Paris and Berlin, and makes a
specialty of the pianoforte. She is 21
years old and Mr. Landsberger is 32.
MRS. DORA FUHRIG'S TRIAL.
The work of securing a jury to try Mrs.
Dora Fuhrig. the notorious midwife, for
causing the death of Mrs. Anna W.
Walmsley, was concluded yesterday morn
ing, the following talesmen being selected:
R. S. Mapleson, Joe Pohelm. S. Gillespie,
J. T. Brophy, A. Binslay, M. H. Mohr
drick. E. P. Enright, T. Frahur. James E.
Dealey, Jacob Wand, M. Spiro and Robert
Cleary.
At the opening of the afternoon session
Mrs. B. H. Gerder, the dead woman's
mother, was placed on the stand. She told
the story of her daughter's suffering and
gave other details or her illness tending
to incriminate Mrs. Fuhrig. When Mrs.
Gerder left the stand the case went over
until this morning.
